it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voicfe,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.

O R SBL V B SI
We wish it to be understood that the character of th
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices vvi
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessing
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stand-
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is wmrthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
Bomewhiie baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

"So cialistic Georye”: A Sketch.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out along the Queensland border where thegidya-
scrub is dense,
And the rabbits swarm in thousands through the
breaches in the fence ;
VV here the waterholes are empty and the stock of
hunger dies,
And you have to share your damper with the bull-
dog ants and flies—
You might chance to meet a spectre on some God-
forgotten track
Wearing spectacles and boxer with a lib’ry on his
back.
It is “Socialistic Georgie”—Western bushmen
call him so—
And I easy recognised him, for he quickly let me
know
That he’d mastered Social-Science and had found
the hidden key
To the old and vexed problem which has kept thd
world “at sea.”
And in mighty learned language he explained to
me his plan
How to better the condition of the starving work-
ing man.
“Do away with priest and monarch, hurl the Fat-
man to his doom,”
And his voice grew husk and hollow, like an echo
from a tomb;
“ Take away from men Ambition !’’ he went on in
louder tone,
“And their crave for empty Mammon ! and the
rest is easy shown.
They will cultivate the country and live happy on
the soil,
And will equally, like brothers, reap tin* harvest
of their toil!”
Then he opened up his bundle, which contained a
lot of books
Most delightfully entangled with his fishing-lines
and hooks.
Bellamy and Herbert Spencer, Henry George, and
many more
Writers on the social questions of the present and
of yore,
He pulled out with great excitement, while I
watched his swag with care,
For I feared he might have hidden dynamite and
powder there. . >
“ I have read all these and often cursed the
author’s work and name,”
He exclaimed, and then a shudder shook his lean
and lanky frame.
And he jumped upon his lib’ry ; gave his starving
dog a kick ;
And “ performed ” in such a manner that I
grabbed a mnlga-stick
To defend with it my carcase, for he filled my
heart with fear,
In that lonely place and dreary, where no human
help was near.
“All those books are impositions—impositions,
nothing more!”
He remarked when he had finished yelling out for
blood and gore.
And, in milder accents, muttered: “Famous
though the authors be,
They don’t understand the question, haven’t
studied it like me ;
And before you’ll be much older, take my word
for it, young man,
You will see a happy nation freed from slav’ry by
my plan !
The December sun was setting and the gidya-
scrub grew dark,
And the jackass started laughing when I ventured
to remark :
“I admit your plan is splendid and your wishes
from the heart,
But you really never mentioned How and Where
you mean to start
Your well-meaning reformation of a billion
diff’rent minds,
And to work these ‘ grab-all ’ people on your
socialistic lines,”
He considered for a minute, then he called his dog
by name,
And packed up his books and papers and walked
back the way he came.
And I s’pose he keeps on wand’ring aimlessly
along the road,
With his poodle-dog and bundle and his literary
load. |
In my dreams I often see him with his hat upon
his swag,
Talking socialistic science to his empty tucker-
bag.
Bourke.
6x8.
Tory: “Protection reduces prices—to
famine. Good boots, in Melb., 45.”
For rheumatism use Oolemane and Sons
Euoalypte Special Oil.
Dear Bulletin, —At Charleston, Etheridge
goldfield (N.Q.), may be seen a reputed black
leper minus one hand and part of one foot. The
white population number about 70, and their sole
water-supply during the dry season is the soakage
from a dam situate on the river above the town-
ship. On the soft sand of the river-bed about 30
blacks are camped, among them the above-men-
tioned patient. Allowing 10 dogs to every black
(rather under than over the average), this would
give about 300 mangy, half - starved, yelping
curs, which, along with the blacks, have free
access to the water used by the residents. The
blacks are, in fact, employed by the majority
of the whites as water-carriers. Inform the
police? Might as well inform the Emperor of
China, of whose subjects there are, I might add,
about 20, some fossicking and some gardening. In
this same township was recently to be seen the
edifying spectacle of an alleged woman, the worse
for drink, parading the street with handcuffs on,
and leg-irons attached to her ankles trailing
behind her. She had made her escape while the
policeman in charge had gone to a neighboring
township for a J.P. to hear the charge against
her.—T. M‘G., Charleston, May 9, ’95.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
££ TIHIIRIEIE CASTLES” and “ C-A-IPST-A-ZCsT.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO.
[Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every, week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confident ial.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in lilacle and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal :~The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also ;— lt. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
S. Australia, W. Australia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Governments on Interprovincial News-
papers, the annual subscription outside N.S. Wales is
now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JUNE 29, 1895.
The Deadlock.
The New South Wales Council has cast out
the Land and Income Tax Assessment Bill
by 41 fossils against 4—two of the four being
the Government’s own representatives, of
whom one believed very little in the measure,
and the other (Attorney-General Want)
believed not at all. Consequently, an ex-
ceedingly complicated situation has arisen,
for which the great system of Government
by Contradiction offers no adequate remedy,
and no remedy that is even moderately
inadequate The Council was apparently
actuated by a number of different motives in
arriving at its almost unanimous decision.
Four years ago the province sent a strong
Protectionist majority to the Assembly, and
when that majority passed the Customs
Duties Bill the present Government party
called on the Upper House, by petition, and
on many public platforms, and through the
press, to squash the will of the people and
cast the Bill into oblivion. The Council did
not follow the advice then, for its personal
interests were not attacked and it was not
roused to any kind of enthusiasm, but it
probably felt a certain pleasure in acting
pow exactly as the present Government
urged it to act a few years ago. It was pro-
bably further guided by the fact that the
Land and Income Tax Bill was avowedly
intended to supply a gap in the Customs
revenue, and that gap being obviously non-
existent under Reid’s proposed revenue
tariff the alleged necessity for the measure
was not so apparent as it might have
been. It was aggravated, also, by the
bellicose attitude of the Premier, whose
avowed desire for a fight with the Upper
House has been so much advertised of late ;
and it was enraged by the still more bellicose
attitude of the Attorney-General. All these
matters combined probably accounted for
2 per cent., or about that much, of the
opposition, and the other 98 per cent, may
safely be set down to the narrowness,
Toryism, and class bigotry of the Council,
which doesn’t want a land-tax because it is
mainly a House of Landholders, and objects
to an income-tax because its own income is
much above the average.
The first result of the Council’s action is
that the legislative work of the year is prac-
tically lost. The Local Government Bill is
already gone : the Premier himself aban-
doned it rather than sacrifice the principle
of giving the large landholder a maximum
of 20 votes at municipal elections. The
Land and Income-tax Assessment Bill has
just been wrecked by a ten-to-one majority.
The Income-tax Bill now before the Assem-
bly is thus rendered purposeless and alto-
gether useless. The new Tariff Bill must
apparently be abandoned for this session if
Premier Reid really believes, as he asserts,
that it will so seriously reduce the revenue
as to make the Land and Income-tax abso-
lutely necessary to fill the void. Under
these circumstances very little remains
except much Hansard and the smellful
memory of the Dibbs-Parkes coalition
to show that there has been any session at
all. And it is more than possible that the
action of the Council may lead to a prema-
ture dissolution or to a hurried prorogation,
in which case all the other unfinished legis-
lation.will go overboard, and the session
will leave behind it no visible results what-
ever. The vehicle of Government by Con-
tradiction gets along with great difficulty at
all times, and when it meets with a very
small obstacle it breaks down altogether.
Then there is generally a dissolution, which,
in the legislative system, answers much the
same purpose as the private citizen’s habit of
going on a “drunk” when his perplexities
become overwhelming. The “drunk”
doesn’t help to unravel things in any way,
neither does the dissolution, but they are
both regarded in certain walks of life as the
orthodox thing under the circumstances.
• « «
Sydney Telegraph demands that the
Reid Government should dissolve Parlia-
ment and appeal to the country for a dis-
tinct and unmistakeable “ mandate” to carry
the Land and Income Tax at all hazards, and
then come back with a huge majority, and
introduce the measure afresh. Considering
that the present system of Government
furnishes no remedy whatever for the
difficulty now on hand, this proceeding
would be about as wise and about as
idiotic as any other. The Reid party got
a huge majority at last election, and has it
still. It also got a most distinct mandate,
in the matter of the land-tax at -all events,
and the Council has jeered at its majority
and jumped on its mandate with both feet.
If it gets another majority and another
mandate, there is absolutely nothing to pre-
vent the Council repeating the process.
Moreover, it would almost certainly come
back with a much weaker following than it
now possesses. It would go to the country
with the pathetic acknowledgment that it
had achieved practically nothing, and the
average elector only looks at results and
takes very little account of causes —so the
Government which does nothing is lost.
Even when the Minis ry has accomplished
much, instead of having a record of almost
utter blankness, the country is generally in-
furiated because it has not done much more.
Therefore, when a Ministry appeals to the
electors after serving its full Parliamentary
term, it is rejected almost as a matter of
course, and its opponents come back
with a big majority. It it dissolves after
serving only part of its term, it either
comes back with a s nail majority or a small
minority —the matter depends very much on
its length of service. It is a mere matter of
depreciation, like the wearing out of any
other machine. If the Reid Cabinet goes
to the country now it ought, in the ordinary
course of things, to come back with its
majority reduced by one-half, and with a
much less forcible mandate than it had
before; and the Council, which refused to be
overawed by its largo majority, would have
still less respect for its little one.
* * *
The next alternative is to take no notice
of the disaster, and to go on with whatever
business it may be possible to accomplish.
Premier Turner did this when the Vic-
torian Council rejected his Land-tax Bill,
but the circumstances were different. Tur-
ner’s “mandate” was practically* to square
the finances by any possible means, and his
land-tax was a matter of convenience rather
than of principle ; therefore, when it was
rejected, he proceeded to raise revenue by
other means, and accomplished his object.
Moreover, the Victorian Council is an elec-
tive body with a property qualification, and
though this renders it as Tory as that of N.S.
Wales, it also puts it so utterly beyond
Governmental control that no Ministry is
supposed to be affected by anything that
happens in the Upper House. In N.S.
Wales the Ministry has the power to ask the
Governor to allow it to appoint new Coun-
cillors, and by virtue of this semblance of
authority it is supposed to “do something ”
when the Council treats it and the country
with unusual disrespect. In nine cases
out of ten it can’t do anything at all,
and in 99 cases out of 100 it can’t do any-
thing of any real value. Still, if it fails to
do something, in such an extreme case as
the present, the Council grows more and
more arrogant until the position becomes
untenable, and the Lower House and the
people begin to view the Ministry with con-
tempt. In this instance, the Upper House
has knocked the bottom and all four sides
out of the entire Government policy, and if
it can’t be suppressed then the chief reason
for the Government’s existence vanishes
altogether.
The only visible remedy for the deadlock
is to swamp the Council with new nominees.
This can only be done by the permission of
the Queen’s shadow--the Governor, or, as
there is no Governor, by the aid of his
deputy shadow. The latter, it is almost
certain, will decline so great a responsibility,
and the former, when he arrives, will prob-
ably do likewise, as most other Governors
have done, unless under extreme pressure,
or by direction of the Imperial Government.
The authority of the Imperial Government
can probably be obtained by sufficiently
enthusiastic representations and after much
delay ; but even in such a case, and suppos-
ing the Ministry lives long enough to see the
end of the struggle, very little good will
have been done if the Council is swamped
merely for the sake of carrying the Land-tax
Bill, and for no other purpose. Out of
the 40 or 50 Democrats who must be ap-
pointed to man down the present Ti ry
majority, not ten would remain democrats
two years hence. The Upper House is
fatal to democratic sentiment. The atmo-
sphere of wealth, ease, and profound respect
for Property, the absence of any necessity to
keep abreast of the age, the feeling of irre-
sponsible authority, the freedom from all
need to consider public sentiment—which
freedom presently developes into a lofty
contempt for public sentiment and every-
thing appertaining thereto, and the undis-
guised dislike of the Assembly for the nomi-
nee House—which speedily induces a feeling
of antagonism even in the man who has
spent many years in the popular Chamber,
all these things make such a thing as a demo-
cratic M.L.O. almost impossible Moreover,
the Council is distrusted by the people, as it
well deserves to be, and cordially returns
the feeling ; and the Democrat who outers
it is distrusted because he is there and
presently learns to resent the popular
suspicion. If the Reid Government, after
many months of conflict with the Imperial
authorities, is allowed to appoint, say, 50
new Councillors, and then rush along its
Land and Income Tax Bill before they have
time to fall from grace, the measure will,
doubtless, be passed. And if that bill
represented the beginning, middle and end
of the country’s aspirations, this result would
be perfectly satisfactory. But the country
desires to keep progressing. It wants many
more things now, and will want still more
later on. And the new members will fossi-
lise just as did the old ones, many of whom
were also men of liberal ideas before they
entered the mephitic atmosphere that per-
vades the House of Old Age, and in another
year or two the ancient barrier will have
become as impassable as ever.
• * *
The Legislative Council should be swamped
for one purpose only. There is nothing to
be gained by dissolving the Assembly, which
has done its best to carry out the popular will.
There is also nothing to be gained by letting
the matter rest, which is merely postponing
the inevitable ; or by prorogation, which is
also postponing the inevitable. And there
is very little to be gained by fighting the
Council on this one point only. The most
statesmanlike course is to introduce into the
Assembly a Bill to reform the Upper House
by converting it into am elective body
appointed on a manhood suffrage basis by
the whole province voting as one constitu-
ency ; or otherwise to amend its constitution
so as to bring it into line with the popular
sentiment. When that measure has passed the
Assembly and been contemptuously thrown
out by the Council, then it will be time to
appeal to the people and get a direct vote on
that question alone—to make it the whole
political platform of the party, on the suffi-
cient ground that all other reforms of any
importance are practically hopeless until this
persistent obstruction is removed from the
path. And, having got the support of the
nation, then it would be the business of the
Government to besiege the viceroy, and the
Colonial Office, and the British Parliament,
until it secured the right to swamp the Coun-
cil with a democratic majority which could be
relied upon to abolish itself and its fellow
nominees for ever and ever. This is the
only question between the two Houses which
is worth fighting. The point on which the
Government should make a stand is not
that the Council has no moral right to veto
the popular decision in the matter of the
Land and Income Tax Bill, but that it has
no moral right to veto the popular decision
in anything, and, not having the power, its
possession of the power to do so is an
anomaly and a danger to the commonwealth.
Some day or other this matter must be dealt
with, and it may as well be done now as at
any other season.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Slaves of the Sugar Industry.
The Opposition in Q. Parliament is numerically
too small to have any practical status. It is
overshadowed, on the one hand, by a Labor
majority, pledged to a definite platform of
“ Socialism in our time,” and, on the other, by
a greater Government majority, upholding a
definite policy of spoils m their time. Charles
Powers, junior member for Maryborough, has
acted as nominal leader of this nominal Opposi-
tion, and may still be regarded as its mouth-
piece. The recent speech in which Vie laid
down the policy of the Progressive Democratic
party in the Northern province lias been re
ceived with attention no less profound than
merited. Powers is entitled to respect by his
intellectual ability, anil still more by his intel-
lectual honesty. As a party-leader he was a
modified failure under circumstances which
made failure certain. His brains are more
conspicuous than his tact : he can neither bully
nor wheedle. But friends and foes are con-
vinced of his sincerity ; and his public career
has done no dishonour to his native Aus-
tralia. There are four main points in his party
programme : electoral reform, white labour,
industrial development, and equal opportunities
given to all citizens by democratic administra-
tion. In his Maryborough speech, Powers
laid most stress on the urgent need for the
exclusion of colored labour, and quoted these
significant figures :
Death-Rate per 1000.
1890— White population .. 12*81 Kanaka .. 46*44
1891 „ „ .. 12*77 ~ .. 61*74
1892 „ „ .. 12*06 „ .. 42*74
1893 „ „ .. 12-54 x .. 52*57
1894 Registrar-General's report
notreo iveil; Bmidaberg dis-
trict (2800 Kanakas there) ~ .. 60*71
These figures are official and unchallengeable ;
yet they do not give the full force of the con-
trast. For the Kanakas are all men in the
prime of life, while the total for whites is
swollen by the inclusion of infants and
aged people—whose mortality is the heaviest
item on every list. Thus, “ summed up,” says
Powers, “ this means that we are putting
these picked men of the islands under ground
at the rate of more than one islander for every
day in every year since the traffic started.” No
wonder the islands are depopulated ! There is
“ no fuss, no funeral notice, no burial service,
nothing to shock the delicate susceptibilities of
a Christian community,” when the Leperland
Kanaka is thrust into his hole. His death is
indeed supposed to be reported to the Regis-
trar ; but a Parliamentary return shows that, in
addition to the reported deaths, there have been
3240 Kanaka disappearances. Where those miss-
ing Kanakas are, nobody knows ; but everybody
can guess. They came ; they should have gone
back, but did not. Doubtless the great majority
of them are buried at the root of Leperland’s
flourishing sugar industry. Yet the Kanaka
death-rate was the ground for only one count
in Powers’ indictment of colored labor. He
added up the leprosy, the prostitution of white
women, the mysterious crimes, the disclosures
of the Wm. MaNson trial, the proof that
unaided white labor could grow* and was grow-
ing cane profitably, the truth that the Kanaka
is but paving a way for the Javanese, the
Japanese and the Indian coolie ; and he asked
young Australia whether it would, with eyes
open, offer up race and country as a sacrifice to
Mammon. Powers’ argument lost no force, but
rather gained it, from the fact that five years
ago he advocated an extension of the term
allowed for colored labor. Five years ago
many men halted doubtfully between a know-
ledge that the Kanaka w'as a danger to
the community and a belief that he could be
temporarily utilised for the benefit of the com-
munity without much danger. But the experi-
ence of the intervening years has convinced
every one of those men, whose mind is not
dominated by personal interest or class pre-
judice, that colored labor is even now' dearly
bought; that in the future (in the words of
Griffith) “it is impossible for an inferior
servile class and a free white laboring popula-
tion to permanently exist side by side ;” and
that if the present policy be continued the
growing evil must result in national degrada-
tion and disaster. Every man who now votes
for colored labor, or for the Govt, which sup-
ports colored labor, has done his best to earn his
children’s bitter curses.
The Man from Brummagem.
Dear Bulletin, —The reign of Brummagem
continueth on Australian shores. The intelli-
gent foreigner is as anxious as ever to ex-
plain how Australia ought to be governed ;
and he is, as you have shown over and
over again, violently willing to take part
in the government of the unfortunate “colo-
nial,” at a salary. Our latest visitor, Mr.
Michael Davitt, tells us, amidst thunderous
applause, that the British working-man will
never give up Freetrade. This secured for him
the whole-souled support of the Melb. Argus,
which has applied that to mean that the example
ought not to be lost on the Australian working-
man. I don’t suppose Mr. Davitt intended to
give an opinion on local politics ; at any rate,
let us hope he differs from the average visitor,
and has no burning desire to set us right.
Some four years ago a Mr. H. H. Champion
arrived in Australia in the middle of a strike,
and immediately saw through the whole
of the intricacies of the shearing, the seamen’s,
and the miners’ war, and had a remedy at once.
The men were all wrong. Their leaders were
all asses. Recently Mr. John Burns visited
the U.S., stayed about three weeks, discovered
that the whole American State, National and
Municipal system, was rotten, depraved and
corrupt, and bestrode the various platforms
with a remedy in each pocket. Mr. Champion is
now about again with fresh ideas as to how we
should be governed. Having failed to get in for
a Scotch constituency to the Commons, having
failed as an editor of the Labor Elector, and as
an agitator, a soldier, and in various other capa-
cities in Great Britain, of course he is singularly
well-adapted to run the province of Victoria-
in fact, the continent of Australia. Naturally,
he seeks a seat in Parliament, on the
strength of six months’ residence. He
is, of course, competent to criticise the
Trades Hall, the Government, institutions
generally. One can only wonder at the
impudence of these people. Suppose I
invited you, sir, to my house, and you had
not been there a quarter of an hour before you
found fault with my whisky; suggested that the
piano should be re-tuned, and that tke picture
of my angel mother should be hung higher
or lower ; criticised the principle upon which 1
was training up my children ! What would
you think of such a thing in sober moments ?
It would be all very ill-bred and very
cheeky. Yet we are constantly finding
ourselves called to task politically and socially
by the. flotsam and jetsam of Europe. Can it
be that we bear such an absolute resemblance to
the Britishers that the absence of any national
trait of recognition about us makes us the prey
of these cock-sure travellers? Have we, in


one hundred years, developed no national
characteristic which cannot be revised out of us
bv the newest arrival on the Orient boats!
After all, I think we prefer rather to go our
own way and solve our own problems than to
depend upon the haughty services of the proud
descendants of the Urquharts. Why Mr.
Champion, before his English boots are yet
worn out, is to be held up as a political example
to the detriment of able Australians is not
apparent. I suggest respectfully, in answer to
certain paragraphs rather in his favor in your
columns recently, that if he was not good
enough for Aberdeen he is not good enough for
Victoria, and that’s not saying much for Vic-
toria. They are a canny people in Aberdeen,
and they know the Urquharts (from whom
Mr. Champion is descended) better than we do.
Victoria can do its own governing without
the aid of the decayed Scotch aristocracy. It
is better to govern ourselves, however badly,
•than to give our governing out to do to men
from Brummagem, Aberdeen, or Cork. —Yours,
Cornstalk.

Grog.
The sitting of Sydney Licensing Court last
week brought the bad-liquor question again to
the front, and gave the law a chance to stumble
to certain illogical conclusions thereon, and to
shed some additional gloom on a very awkward
problem. One of the peculiarities of the law is
that it generally does its best to avoid responsi-
bility, and, in its desire to do this, it often
makes an ass of itself. A large number
of licenses were objected to on the ground that
the holders had been convicted of selling liquor
that was unfit for human consumption, and the
Bench thereupon tried to take a comprehensive
view of the situation—said situation being
somewhat as follows: (1) When a publi-
can is convicted of selling grog that
no human being should be allowed to
drink, it is recognised that his license
should be refused at the next opportunity, and
that he should be shunted out of the trade
for the public good ; (2) the law holds
the publican responsible whether the said
liquor was compounded by him, or by the
European exporter, or the local wholesale
dealer, and whether he knew it was bad or not;
(3) the law fixes no recognised standard of good-
ness, so liquor is approved or condemned by the
public analyst according to a standard of his
own, which may or may not be correct; (4) the
wholesale dealer may sell, with almost absolute
impunity, any kind of liquor that he pleases,
and, owing to the want of supervision in his
case, no one can tell who is responsible for bad
liquor. The Court considered these points
to the best of its moderate ability—
and renewed the licenses of most of the
alleged adulterators. Almost all of them
advanced the usual pleas that they hadn’t made
the bad liquor, or bought it from any illicit
distiller, but had gob it from a reputable firm,
and sold it in the same condition as received,
and that the wholesaler was the only person
to blame. The police-court authorities always
ignore these pleas ; the Licensing Court, which
was probably just about as confused in the
head as the other court, either believed them,
or it held that it didn’t matter whether the
public was poisoned or nob, or else it recognised
the obvious fact that it had no reliable informa-
tion either way. In any case, its decision
means that somebody—whether the wholesale
or the retail dealer, or the exporter, or the
illicit manufacturer, or all combined—are to
go on poisoning the public very much as here-
tofore. The court couldn’t but be con-
vinced that an enormous amount of bad
liquor is traded off to corrode the pub-
lic liver and burn large holes in the
national stomach ; the evidence showed that
conclusively, and if the court possesses the
sense of taste it probably knew it without the
evidence. By renewing the licenses in all save
a few very glaring cases, it simply acknow-
ledged itself unequal to the occasion. It re-
cognised that the bad liquor business can’t be
abolished by sitting hard down, whether rightly
or wrongly, on the ignorant publican, and the
law has never discovered any way of sitting
effectively on other persons concerned, or even
of finding out who is concerned. And it never
will find a way until the State runs the business
itself, and is its own importer and wholesale and
retail dealer, and abolishes the illicit distiller
by abolishing the private publican who buys
his compounds, and fixes the responsibility
once for all by being itself directly and
indirectly responsible for everything. The
Licensing Court didn’t say that or anything
so unconventional, but it did its best to prove
it none the less. The whole interminable
series of prosecutions for selling bad liquor,
illicit liquor, selling liquor after hours, selling
liquor on Sundays, selling liquor to intoxicated
persons and the like, all help to prove that the
State can’t make the private grog trade go
straight, though it spends half its valuable time
in the attempt, und that the whole business
wants refixing on a new basis.
Class- Taxation.
The N.S. W. Council assigns as one reason—if
not the chief reason—for its rejection of the
land-tax, that the Bill submitted to it pro-
vided an exemption up to .£175. The holder of
land of that or less value was to go absolutely
free, therefore the measure was so obviously
one of “ class-taxation ” that the Council re-
jected it with loathing. Since then, a number
of the supporters of the Bill have been em-
ployed in an endeavor to prove that the land-
tax scheme, despite the £475 exemption, is not
class-taxation, and a lot more have apologised
for the Bill on the ground that it is a
good Bill upon the whole, and, though the
exemption is a blot upon it, the blot can be
easily removed. These two parties represent
two sections of land-taxers, and The Bulletin
now desires to speak on behalf of a third. The
Land Tax Bill, with the £475 exemption, is a
measure of class taxation. Also, it ought t > be
one, and it was distinctly intended to be > ie,
or if it wasn’t so intended it should have been.
And the laind-taxer who apologises for this
feature in the Bill, or who offers to sacrifice it
in the interests of alleged equity, lacks the
courage of his opinions. The land-tax is not
a gospel, as so many of its advocates
maintain, but an expedient for the public
good. It isn’t wanted on any principle of
abstract justice, or because of the hollow cant
phrase that “ all things come from the land,”
or for any reasons to be found in the Scripture ;
the only cause for its existence is that the pub-
lic welfare demands it. It is necessary for the
public good, either that the land should belong
to the State so that the “ unearned increment ”
may be public property, or else that the laud
should be divided up on something like an
equal basis, so that the said increment
may be shared among the nation with
a reasonable semblance of equality. The
possession of vast estates by the few re-
duces the many to landlessness, poverty,
and something very much akin to slavery,
consequently it is advisable, for the good of the
State, that something approaching to equal
distribution should be arrived at. It matters
very little how it is arrived at, so long as the
nation ultimately gets there. Land nationalis-
ation would produce the result. So, to some
extent at least, would the French law of in-
heritance. The Reid Government’s land-tax
was intended—or should have been—for the
same purpose, and to effect that purpose it
must be an impost on a class only. If the small
holding is exempt while the large one is heavily
taxed, then it becomes more profitable to
divide the soil among a multitude of small
cultivators, and a process of subdivision sets in
which tends to destroy the big estates and to
break up the land monopoly. If all land is
taxed equally, and there is no exemption what-
ever, then there is no encouragement towards
sub-division, and the big estates will remain ;
and if the land impost isn’t class taxation, then
there is very little reason, beyond the fact that
the Treasury wants money, why there should
be any such impost at all. It is not, or at all
events it shouldn’t be, so much a tax on wealth
as a fine levied on the man who grabs more than
his fair share of the country, and thereby com-
pels someone else to go short to the general
injury of the community. The ,£475 exemption
may not be mathematically accurate, but the
principle on which it is based is indisputable.
Also, if there had been no exemption, the Leg.
Council would have thrown out the Bill because
there wasn’t one.
Law in Leperland.
The Q. Full Court, consisting of Justices
Harding, Chubb, and Real, has unanimously
upheld the judgment of Chief Justice Griffith
in the case Browne v. Cowley ; and the lega-
lity of the decision may therefore be taken as
proved. As already stated, the plaintiff, a
member of Q. Parliament, sued the Speaker for
damages for forcibly expelling him from his
place in the Assembly, and preventing him from
re-taking it during a week. The Speaker based
his action on a vote of the Assembly, itself
based on an Assembly standing order. As a
matter of fact, Browne was disgracefully used,
and there was nothing to justify the ill-usage.
The questions put before the court were (1)
Was the standing order valid, and (2) Had the
Govt, majority a right to apply it in Browne’s
particular case ? The Court declined to con-
sider the second question, and held that the
standing order (passed by practically the
same Govt. majority) was valid. Into
the law points raised it is unnecessary to
enter. The resulting position is this: The
Govt, majority may prevent any opposing
member from sitting in the House for so long
as it pleases, by simply voting his expulsion.
He has no redress. His election counts for
nothing. He may be expelled for any offence
or for no offence whatever. If his presence is
unpleasant to an Assembly majority, the elec-
tors whom he represents may be altogether dis-
franchised —simply blotted out of the scheme
of representative Government. In other words,
if, out of the 71 effective members of Q.
Assembly, 36 can at any time be found un-
scrupulous enough to vote that the remaining
35 be expelled, they can do it without
showing cause by merely passing a resolution.
The minority will have no right to be heard in
self-defence, no right to reason or protest. The
majority can, without discussion, over-ride the
constitutional rights of the minority; and the
Supreme Court of the province holds that the
majority will be quite within its powers. No
more monstrous position is imaginable. Natur-
ally, the Government has not proposed to take
any steps to alter it. Nelson and Co. acquiesce
cheerfully in a decision which may be used to
tighten their grip of office. Nothing less was
expected from the most viciously partisan
administration of Australasia. Premier Nelson,
bound by his position to do equal political jus-
tice to all members of the community, is now
President of the Q. National Ass, with the
man BulcoCk as his secretary. He actually has
the insolence to issue a circular, signed by him-
self as President, pointing out that the Ass has
been formed to combat the progress of the
Labor-party and soliciting support. That is to
say, he prostitutes the office and the dignity
entrusted to him as head of the Govt, in order
to advance the interests of a class-organisation
which is now doing its utmost, by hook and
crook, to take away the franchise from all
electors suspected of Democratic tendencies !
The process of “ bulcocking ” the rolls is
steadily going forward —with the Govt, aiding
and abetting. If there were in Leperland any
public honesty, any public opinion worth the
name, such conduct would rouse a storm of in-
dignation. There is heard hardly a word of
remonstrance ! The province is drugged into
torpor.
Hebraism and Christendom.
Julian Emanuel Salomons, Q.C., must have
felt ecstatically humorous when he found him-
self not only placed among the notables on
Cardinal Moran’s lecture-platform, but even
selected as the champion of Roman Catholicism,
to move a vote of thanks for the Cardinal’s
singularly barren address on the “ Reunion of
Christendom.” Salomons projected himself at
the audience “ as one of thousands of friendly
survivors of the ancient race who had long for-
gotten and altogether forgiven the injustice of
the past.” This venom-tipped arrow launched,
the great little barrister, while admitting that
he could not give an unqualified assent to
everything Moranesque that he had heard,
deplored “ the dissensions which split up the
Christian Church into contending factions.”
The gall that could fling this touch of Hebraism
at a (chiefly) R.C. audience, and the den-
sity of the R.C. audience that could take such
sentiment seriously, are specially worthy of
each other. When, however, Julian Emanuel
further deplored that “ all that raises life above
the level of the brute is being assailed by those
to whom nothing is sacred,” and spoke of
“ masses of festering corruption, a general low
level of conduct, an almost impossibility of
getting the truth in courts of justice ” (and
Royal Commissions?), as reasons why Christen-
dom should be reunited ; even the magnetised
audience breathed hard, said sotto voce, “ Well,
he ought to know,” and thought of lots of things.
As if to throw Salomons into a relief of lime-
light, comes the comment of his fellow-Hebrew,
Rabbi Goldreich, of Ballarat (Vic.), who
doesn’t “forget,” nor does he seem to “for-
give,” dla Salomons. The Rabbi grunts that
“the poor Jew ” is left out of the proposed “Re-
union,” left to the tender mercies of so-called
Christian governments “to trample him under-
foot with savage barbarity, while enlightened
and humane Governments stand aghast, or, at
least, pretend to stand aghast, according cheap
sympathy, with folded arms, and at best afford
him stepmotherly recognition.” Yet (contends
the Rabbi) the Jew is the only dispenser of
Catholic or universal salvation. The Jew gave
the world the Bible, the Christ, and the
Apostles, and still preaches universal salvation
in his synagogues the one only Catholic
religion. And the various Christian sects are
unknowingly fighting with each other in order
to disseminate and teach the Jewish com-
pendium of revelation ! The attitudes of these
two notable Hebrews are well worth contrast-
ing. The oily gammon of Salomons, Q.C.,
and the hen-with-chickens assumption of Pastor
Goldreich!
As a supplement to the article in last issue,
The Bulletin herewith gives a statement of
“information wanted” and not supplied in
Prof. Morris’s memoir of Higinbotham, in the
sure and certain hope that it may be resur-
rected :
1. Higinbotham’s artistic, dramatic, literary,
and musical studies, tastes, and opinions.
2. His views on Berry and the Embassy.
3. His views oh the Strong Persecution, and his
attitude towards the so-called “Australian
Church.”
4. His views on Imperial titles.
5. Some true accounts of the stormy scenes at
Exhibition Commission meetings, whereat the
dignified democrat was baited by the bumptious
emissaries of Flinders-lane.
6. His sources of political inspiration and
thought.
7. His opinions of prominent contemporaries.
8. Any vigorous anecdotes of his career, those
of the sanctified Sunday-school-superintendent
genus rigorously excepted.
9. His views on capital punishment.
10. His matured views on Socialism.
In connection with the first item, The Bulle-
tin believes that Higinbotham originated the
proposal to import Cowen and his orchestra,
and knows that he was a constant attendant at
the high-class concerts that followed thereon.
Also, it knows that he, on one occasion, justified
the reading of fiction, and condemned the
French realistic novel. Furthermore, it has a
dim recollection that he seldom, if ever, visited
a theatre. But it is information much more
full-bodied than this that is wanted. The list
given by no means exhausts the list of absent
ana. It will, however, do for a preliminary
stimulus to the resurrection of a substantial
body for the Big Book that is to be.
The Dean Commission has plainly shown that
there is something seriously wrong with the
present New South Wales system of taking
notes at a trial. Even in the interests of the
Judges a change is desirable. Not only is the
mere mechanical labor involved in writing at
high speed throughout the day very great, but
it must inevitably prevent the Judge from
getting a proper grasp of the case as a whole.
No Judge can reasonably be expected to edit a
case and report it also. And when Judges sit,
as too often happens at the Central Criminal
Court, for 10 hours at a stretch, the system
becomes positively dangerous. Often, when
counsel comes to deal with something vital to
his case, it turns out that the point on
which he relies does not appear upon the
Judge’s notes. The Judge, having no idea
that it was likely to prove important, did not
take it down, and has no recollection of it, and
so counsel is left helpless. The police-courts
teach the remedy, which is simple. A clever
type-writer should take down every word of
every witness unless otherwise directed. Both
sides would then know precisely what evidence
is before the court. The Judge could still take
private notes for his own guidance and assist-
ance, but on doubtful points the notes of the
clerk would be authoritative and final.
Ed. Bulletin, —“Justice” confounds cause
and effect when he ascribes the defects in Aus-
tralasia’s prison system to the fashion in which
N.S. Wales warders are treated. It is like
saying that an ill-working machine would go
properly, provided it were lubricated with
better oil. This entirely misses The Bulle-
tin’s point, which was, as I understand it, that,
first, the machine is bad, and, secondly, it is
badly engineered. Anefficient, specially-trained
and well-treated prison staff certainly is an
essential to the success of a good system. But,
even if the staff be perfect, it can’t do more
than minimise the effects of an evil system. As
a matter of fact, the probabilities are, not that
the staff' will improve the system, but rather
that the system will spoil the staff. At
present no province recognises the semi-scien-
tific aspect of prison arrangement. Conse-
quently, no special qualifications are required
in those to whom the administration is en-
trusted. A prison chief should be something of
a sanitarian, psychologist, physiologist, and
much more than the commonplace civil-servant
that he generally is. Similarly, his subordin-
ates should be trained in their work as
thoroughly as hospital nurses are equipped for
theirs, and be much more than ordinary day
laborers. As soon as tlie prison system becomes
enlightened, so soon will the true status of
prison officials be recognised. Meantime,
nothing can be expected from an administration
that has a gag-advocate at its head and a
hangman and fiagellator at its tail. —P.M.
In answer to Judge Williams’ remarks upon
costs in a Melb. law-court last week, a wigged
craftsman delivered himself of the dreary old
gag that “ these long cases pay counsel worse
than any other cases.” The gag, if true in refer-
ence to a particular case, is an impudent fiction
at heart. A counsel engaged upon a long, try-
ing job may miss an opportunity to deal with
several lucrative little jobs at once, and he thus
earns less in one week, or fortnight, or longer
term, than he could have made by the un-
scrupulous practice of accepting more briefs
than he had time to attend to properly. But
it doesn’t necessarily follow that the “ one-
case ” shark would have been able to swal-
low a shoal of fat little fishes whilst he
was devouring the lengthy but comparatively
unnutritious barracouta. A long case is a sure
and satisfactory income while it lasts, and little
cases are not always very plentiful and very
fat. And, granting that a fashionable barrister
is in constant demand—what keeps him fashion-
able ? The occasional long case, the cause
celebre which “pays worse than any other
case.” Thereby he maintains the prestige
which is the most valuable part of his stock-in-
trade. A barrister who persistently refused to
touch long cases would soon decline in market
price. The truth of the matter is that “ these
long cases pay counsel better (in notoriety,
which means money) than any other cases.”
As a set-off to the enormous groans of Austra-
lian and Maoriiand Tories about the ruin which
is coming to pass through the Democrat policy
of the Seddon Government, the following re-
port by an unbiassed observer —the U.S. Consul
at Auckland—is worth reading :
As to the country having drifted into Socialism, it is
only fair to say that there is very little need of appre-
hension in that respect, at least for the present. At the
same time it cannot be denied that the tendency of legis-
lation appears to be pointing that way. If it be Socialism
to relieve the poor, the working man, the artisan, the
struggling small fanner and the mechanic from the bur-
dens of taxation as much as possible, and compel the
monopolist, the land-grabber, the purse-proud and the
affluent members of society to bear the weight and ex-
pense of Government, then Socialism Is certainly in full
swing here. If it be Socialism to shorten the hours of
the laborer to eight per day, and give him a half-holiday
in every week, besides at least a half-dozen
full holidays in the year under full pay, thus
affording him more time for rest, lecreation,
and intellectual development than is enjoyed by his
fellow-workers in any part of the world, then, indeed, it
is undeniable that Socialism is rampant in Maoriiand. If
it be Socialism to compel the admission of more pure air
and genial sunshine into the workroom and factory,
under Government supervision ; to teach the laborers
their rights, and how to lawfully and peaceably obtain
them ; to force the earth-grabber to either sell, subdivide,
or improve his land so that it will produce what Nature
intended it should, thereby administering to the wants of
the people ; or place the land within the reach of those
who desire homes—if this be Socialism, then indeed are
the people of this country blessed beyond all others, for
all I have enumerated, and more, are they enjoying to the
fullest extent to-day. There is a general diffusion of
wealth, no great poverty, and not a single millionaire so
far as I know. Although legislation does not directly
interfere with the laudable accumulation of thrift and
industry', yet there is no denying that the general ten-
dency is towards checking, if not absolutely preventing,
the acquisition of vast estates in the hands of individuals
or companies to the detrimentof thepeople. This cannot
in any sense be called Socialism. The men who have in-
augurated these honest, Christian reforms are not ani-
mated by any spirit of Socialism, but by a sincere desire
to promote the universal welfare, to resist the aggression
of the strong and lend a helping hand to the weak and
lowly. You may call these principles by any name you
choose, but the facts are as herein related.
♦ .
Dear Bulletin, —The fact that “H” is hn
pressed with the Age correspondent’s idea (a
very ancient one, by the way) of an Australian
penal station on one of the outlying islands only
goes to indicate that “ H.” is oblivious of his-
tory and blind to contemporary circumstances.
First of all, the Norfolk Island experience
ought to be sufficient lesson to last throughout
eternity. Its record of piracies, conspiracies,
escapes, murders, outbreaks, hangings, ami
lashings are the “ preserved fruits ” of island
penal settlements, remote from civilisation and
supervision. St. Helena, the shark-sentinelled
island prison of Queensland, has been con-
demned on account of its remoteness and con-
sequent expensiveness. New Caledonia is
simply an insular Gehenna. In fact, any place
that is solely inhabited by criminals and their
keepers soon becomes an abode of savage bar-
barism and villainy. Even if experience did
not thunder so eloquently against the proposal
“ H.” applauds, the spirit of the new school of
criminology is totally opposed to all ostrich-like
subterfuges on the part of society. Society
would doubtless willingly shirk the problems of
crime by shipping criminals to island-prisons
and making believe that they did not exist.
But you can’t cure a cancer by covering it with
a plaster.— Cop.
An “ Amurrikin ” railway-expert named Pang-
borne recently arrived in Melbourne on a visit
of inspection. Before he had been there two
day he furnished some columns of interview
to the local papers showing exactly how the
Victorian railways ought to be managed and
how they are not managed at present, and
explaining how State ownership was always
extravagant and inefficient compared to “private
enterprise” as practised in the blessed U—nited
States. And the amusing part of the busi-
ness was that the Melbourne papers, with
their usual holy reverence for the foreign
“expert,” insisted on treating Bangiiorn from
Amurrika as somebody of importance,
whose views were to be taken in awful
seriousness, as those of one who spoke with
authority. The country which Banghorn
comes from is par excellence the land of railway
muddlement, mismanagement, bankruptcy,
fraud and confusion. There are 128 bankrupt
railroads in the States, and 42 per cent, of the
U.S. railroads are, or have been, insolvent.
The U.S. private enterprise lines pay smaller
returns than these of Victoria, have a far larger
proportion of accidents, are worked at much
greater expense, are, in many cases, not nearly
so well built, and yet cost much more per mile
for construction. Under which circumstances
an intelligent community should simply have
looked at Pangborn with a pitying smile, and
told him to run home to his mother. Tuere is

“A Scrap of Paper,” which is a sinful
French comedy with the wickedness turned to
harmless mirth, should give a substantial lift to
the Daore season at Her Majesty’s. This pro-
duction may be beet described as an eloquent
essay on the cussedness of inanimate objects.
Two foolish young people correspond clandes-
tinely, using a bronze figure on a bracket as their
post-office. Through a complication of circum-
stances they are separated, and the lady’s last
letter remains for long years in the bowels of the
bronze figure, and the dust and the dead flies
gather upon it. But when she is married to a
jealous baronet, and the old lover comes back and
recovers the incriminating document, she sud-
denly realises the awfulness of the situation. The
girly variety of love-letter is never dated, and
nobody can ever prove, when it turns up in after
life, that it wasn’t written after marriage
instead of before. Thereafter the comedy
is a frantic hunt for the compromising
letter. It is found in a tobacco-box and
scorched, and then left "bn the fender. A man
lights a candle with it, and throws it out of the
window, right at the feet of the jealous husband,
who picks it up abstractedly and lends the remains
of it to a dull old entomologist, to wrap a beetle
in. A precocious youth throws away the beetle,
and writes a hurried love-message on the paper.
An old maid, in ringlets, gets hold of it, and
passes it back to the dazed entomologist. Mr.
Daore gets a dreadful cold looking for it in the
damp grass, and two marriages and various other
things arise out of it. The paper leads a charmed
life until 10.35, when Stirling Whyte dodders
away with the ashes of it'in his candlestick, and
the lost beetle still on his mind.
* # *
The “ Scrap of Paper ” is a bright, fasci-
nating play, and the company treat it in a large
and capable spirit. Miss Amy Roselle reveals
herself as a lady with great capabilities
in comedy. Mr. Dacre, who makes a pic-
turesque entrance under a red umbrella with
p dnted green beasts on it, does
all through, and his pnesent character, when
c unpared to the unfinished, leggy deceiver in last
week’s tragedy, raises his local reputation many
degrees. Zenobia, the spinster with the cork-
screw curls and the baleful spectacles, is finely
rendered by Miss Kate Bishop, and Scot Inglis’s
baronet is about bis happiest effort up to date.
Sundry other people, including a nice girl, and an
old man who is gone in the back and the memory,
and a small boy who wants to fight a duel, also
!*ave a pleasing impression behind them ; and
somebody tells a weird parable, based on tea, to
the effect that you should pour boiling water over
your sweetheart, and then throw it away, in order
to extract the full flavor of the lady.
# # «
Miss Amy Roselle recites “ Rizpah,” all
by herself, as an afterpiece. “Rizpah” is a
dreadful story about Bones. A young man had
been hanged, and remained hanging according to
law until the weather and the crows ate him
away, and the dickey-birds sang on his vertebrae
by day, and his dry remnants clattered on the
gallows upon windy nights. Then at last his
bones began to drop off, and his mother gathered
them up one by one on many lonesome midnights,
and buried them in holy ground. When the last
bone was buried she told her story in verse—and
died. It was a great effort of elocution, and the
audience listened spell-bound. Her Majesty’s has
been growing more and more fashionable of late,
and consequently the hum of conversation is at
times almost as aggressive as in the d.c. of the
Lyceum. But even the most fashionable auditor
stopped talking to listen to that dreadful narra-
tive of Bones.
** * *
Miss Maggie Moore has closed her Sydney
season, and the Irish drama is having a well-earned
rest, and Mr. Oily Deering, the humpy process-
server of “Sweet Innisfail,” is presumably trying
to straighten his back to its ordinary shape. The
Royal is now being painted and re-decorated,
which should do that well-worn house a lot of
good. Also, Rev. Haskett Smith has departed,
and has left a good-sized blank behind. Rev.
Haskett was an interesting personage, and he
disseminated more information during his brief
stay than any other of the Most-Travelled
Smythe’s stars up to date.
*# * *
The “Gaiety Girl” still toils not to any
great extent, neither does she spin to an alarm-
ing degree, at Sydney Lyceum. Careful in-
spection of that fairy-like production leads to the
idea that there is very little meat in it and not
much bone, and whoever desires solid hunks of
literary steak will have to go elsewhere. But, in
its own butterfly fashion, the piece goes well. It
bears the same relation to the ordinary drama
that a perfumed sigh does to 81b. of underdone
meat, and though man cannot live on perfumes
and must needs yearn after coarse, prosaic bull-
beef, yet they are very pleasant now ‘ and then.
Blanche Massey seems to prow colder as time goes
On, and languishes beautifully under the false
accusation like a wronged maiden at the North
Pole. The Bulletin begins to be troubled with
a fear that the lovely Blanche suffers from cold
feet. Grace Palotta doesn’t act any better,
but her smile grows upon the audience
more and more violently every evening. So
does Madge Rossell’s famous dance, and
the gorgeous high kick, and the beautiful respon-
dent. Louis Bradfield is very little seen in this
production—he is not much more than a rumor at
the back of the stage—so Sydney hasn’t yet
formed an opinion of him. But its views concern-
ing Monkhouse and Bantoek are so far favorable,
and the latter doesn’t now come in quite"* such a
moderate second as reported last week. Also,
people snigger quietly to themselves in the street as
they remember Fred. Kaye as the Major. Little
Decima Moore has ifnfortunately been ill, and the
aching void thus created has been about three-
parts filled by Miss Sophie Elliott. Nobody
could quite fill D'eckna’s part except Decima
herself.

Two new artists from America— one of
them a rather pretty lady who goes by the dread-
ful name of Miss Lottie Medley Murphy—appeared

feats with that olde Englyshe weapon.

The variety co. at Sydney Empire seems
to have settled down for a long season. George
Buller, formerly of Dampier’s co., a manager and
actor of high ability, now manages the show, and
a lot of capable comedians are oh the programme.
** * *
“The Masqueraders/' so effectively pro-
duced in Sydney three months ago, is putting in a
week’s run at Melb. Princess’. Up to the end of
the third act the “ The Masqueraders ” is a power-
ful story, although bizarre in construction and
false in sentiment. Henry Arthur Jones always
aims at originality, and hits the unreal, wherefore
his problem plays come and go, and leave no
lasting impression. And because the public feel,
rather than observe, the artificiality of his work
they don’t take it to heart. It is novel, even
startling, but it doesn’t convince. There’s no
enduring truth in the philosophy of H. A. Jones,
nothing but earnest cant and conventional snob-
bishness. Nor is the serious Jones likely ever to
write a really fine play ; since he lacks a sense of
humor with which to detect his own absurdities
and pull himself up short occasionally.
* * *
The heroine of “The Masqueraders” is
introduced to us as a well-bred barmaid who has
never been kissed. Dulcie Larondie is not other-
wise-very admirable. She flirts with an obvious
scamp for the sake of his title and coin. A dreamy
astronomer worships her, and she, with true bar-
maid perversity of instinct, speaks slightingly of
the romantic creature. Being a woman, as well
as a damsel who sells drinks, she is afterwards
prompted by circumstances to kiss her respectful
juggins on his brow. The gentle smack of that
impulsive caress ought to be the keynote of her
character, but it isn’t. Dulcie marries the
scampish baronet'and makes no apparent effort
to reform him. Her bearing towards him is
certainly not influenced by the doctrines of
the marriage-service. The baronet’s spirituous
breath offendeth her nostrils, the baronet bids her
sell her favors, yet matrimony with this noxious
fellow, for whom she never felt a thrill of real
affection, has transformed the reckless girl of the
first act into a paragon of propriety. The astrono-
mer loves her more.than ever. Dulcie draws upon
his bank-account and loves him in return, not
with the flesh-and-blood love of an impulsive bar-
maid, or of a wife driven by harsh treatment to
find a consoler, but with a most respectable love
that can look the marriage-service in the face
without turning an eyelash. Ultimately the
astronomer risks .£200,000 on the even money
chance of winning Dulcie and her child from the
beastly baronet. The bdst two out of three outs
with the cards is to decide it—beastly baronet wins
astronomer’s money or astronomer wins the queen of
his heart, with b.b.’s baby thrown in. The baro-
net didn’t care a curse about his child ; his wife’s
virtue he had been willing to sell for £2OO in the
previous act,/in t now he professed an intense de-
sire to retain his family. He doesn’t say “Give
me £lOO,OOO straight out, and damme ! they are
yours.” He cuts the cards with childish eager-
ness and nervousness impossible in a hardened
sassiety gamester, and turns into a jelly-fish when
fortune goes against him. The gambling scene is
splendidly dramatic, but wholly inconsistent with
facts at the time and afterwards.
* *
There is a fourth act to “ The Masquer-
aders,” where Duloie’s irritating dove-colored
sister, and the astronomer’s demented young
brother come in and hear “the voice of God”
crying that legal prostitution in the arms of a
brandy-scented horror is better than a high moral
love unsanctified by a parson’s blessing. Also,
Dulcie hears the Voice. It persuades the ex-
barmaid that her sweetheart’s reverential embrace
would be “more horrible” than the loathsome
hug of her hioouppy baronet. Likewise, the
astronomer hears the Voice, and rushes away to
his doom, and the voice of Nature moans the
requiem of buried hopes and busted happiness as
the curtain falls. If the dignified clergy don’t
erect a statue to H. A. Jones, they ought to be
ashamed of themselves. The characters of hus-
band and wife are excellently filled by Titheradge
and Miss Olliffe. The new actress can’t be
great in the preposterous last act, but she
glorifies the other three, and her “ yes ”
when asked if she consents to be gambled for is a
stroke of genius. Titheradge is spared inclusion
in the last act. He falls flabby beside the card-
table and quits the drama full of honors and
strong drink. Mr. Elwood’s first appearance, as
the astronomer, was less of a brilliant success, on
account of his monotonous voice vibration. As-
tronomers, however, are not every-day people, so
Mr. Elwood’s voice and manner were probably
suited to the part. Miss Faber, as the dove-
colored sister, conveys the necessary idea that she
is on terms of intimacy with the celestial host,
whilst 15 other people throw side-lights on this
sinful’ world. “The Masqueraders,” as already
remarked, is for a week only. On Saturday the
B. and B. Go. will interpret “The New Woman,”
by Sydney Grundy, for the first time in Aus-
tralia.
“The Fatal Card” appears to be doing
the triek for Bland Holt at Melb. Royal. A well-
written story which opens with a roaring night in
a bar-room, and is finally blown up by an infernal
machine, could hardly fail to attract attention,
apart from its first-class murder, the incident of a
desperado’s escape from Judge Lynch on the
boiler of a passing locomotive, and a naked
comedian paying his addresses to a young lady
with his fishing-rod. Gentle Jim continues to
earn strong marks of disapproval from his com-
panions in crime, whereas Miss Hilda Spong
grows in favor 1 and her latest burst of distress on
Walter* Baker’s account is regarded as the best
specimen of emotion that his sufferings have ever
wrung from her. Miss Spong has in former times
shared a lot of Baker’s grief without seeming
much upset.
** * *
Frederick Villiers, the great war corres-
pondent, has nm his course at Melbourne Bijou,
and gone to other cities, lamented by all who
didn’t hear him. Those who did hear him are
tortured by remembrances of a cast-iron lecturer
telling them nothing worth the hearing concern-
ing matters which he didn’t thoroughly explain,
and this not in honest descriptive English, but in
gingerbread journalese. The variety shows stand
where they stood last week, but the Haunted
Swing was put up to auction recently, and Mac-
Mahon has taken the Kinetoscope for a tour.
The drama is a strong one,
With thrilling scene,
But O ! the villain of the piece
Is wondrous crushed and mean.
In language Ibud and stormy,
By comrades stern and grim,
That person mild is much reviled
They call hvm Gentle Jim.
Way out at Colorado
The villain first is found ;
A lawless mate upbraids him. for
A selfish, crawling hound ;
But with his bold abettors
He’s always in the swim,
And when there’s dirty work to do
They send for Gentle Jim.
In London he is willing
To sneak, at duty’s call,
A pile of bonds from Baker’s dad,
Or like a burglar fall.
And, whilst he’s getting ready,
It is thecaptain’s whim
To signify contempt and scorn
For catspaw Gentle Jim.
He slaughters Baker’s parent,
He lays the old ’uhflat ;
The haughty captain of the gang
Gets on to him for that.
And when the spoil’s divided
Within a cottage dim,
His comrades three they all agree
In loathing Gentle Jim.
Poor villain of the drama !
His lot is truly hard—
The other men are leading trumps,
But he’s “The Fatal Card.”
And when the game is over
We sympathise with him,
Despised and cursed on every hand
Unhappy Gentle Jim.

Any reference to the Jews is strictly forbidden at one
Sydney theatre, the penalty for disobedience being instant
dismissal—which taboo has arisen from the fact that a
few thin-skinned Hebrews object to funny stories con-
cerning the 'Chosen.
Singers have curious whims. The late Vi. Yarley was
very ambitious to sing in Melh. Town Hall, where, she
was convinced, her voice would be heard to better advan-
tage than in a theatre. To gratify her, a concert was to
have been held prior to her trip to England at the end of
the year.

letin predicted her failure. To-day the clever woman is
at her best. Careful observation of all that was worth re-
membering in-London and Paris opera performances
effected a really surprising change in “our Nellie,” and
if her voice holds out she will “ knock ’’ New Babylon
at the first opportunity.

Dear Bulletin,—A “Poverty Point" par. "pokes
borak" at a certain Melb. musical critic who attends
Marshall Hall’s concerts, armed with an appalling score,
and follows the performance note by note. This is not
absurd a spectacle as the ssme critic doing the
same thing at aW. and M. comic-opera show. He might
have been seen forming his opinion of “ Nitouche," for
instance, with the aid of the musical score. Even a ballet
is judged by the orchestra’s fidelity to the blessed book.
One wrong note from a careless cornet would seem of
greater importance to this wise man than 12 beautiful
kicks by the same number of delightful legs.—Yours,
Philistine.

Irving (writes a Bulletin correspondent) made a capital
chairman at the Savage Club annual dinner at Holborn
restaurant. He closed the Lyceum for the night to be
free. He made a really capital, witty speech, and was
unanimously elected a life member. Certainly his chair-
manship at this particular juncture will give a great fillip
to' the old Club. Amongst those present were Poet
Sladen, Gov. Billrobinson, and Phil May. It must
have cost Irving at least £350 to close the Lyceum that
evening.
The Langtry lately appeared in a new play in New
York. In the third act she appeared with “her brow
encircled by a magnificent diamond coronet. Festoons
of diamonds draped the lovely shoulders of the Jersey
Lily." Wrote a critic: “She was as impressive as the
chandelier in the * Boston Theatre,’ which has always
been considered the finest thing in crystal gas-fixtures
ever produced in this world or likely to lie In the next.”

Jones’s, “ Triumph of the Philistines,” &c., at the St.
last week, in which a cocotte (quasi artist’s
model) is shown on the stage unblushingly plying her-
trade? We have seen (writes The Bulletin’s London,
correspondent) New Women and Women with a Past, but;
this is the first time,'so far as I recollect, that such a.,
startler has been exhibited on the stage. No wonder that-
Mr. Jones received such an unflattering reception at the-
end of the play, which, I sincerely trust, may have but a-
short life. Miss Juliette Nesviile wears some very bizarre-
gowns—such as only one of her supposed calling would”
have the hardihood to put on her back.

“ 8.E.” : At the outset you denied the truth of a plain;,
harmless statement of fact. The Bulletin had stated 1 ,
without the least desire to disparage your clever friends,
the acrobats, that something went wrong with the Living
Bridge, at Melb. Royal one night, whereby the “invisible
wire-trick became very visible to the audience.” Mr.
Bland Holt would, of course, have contradicted the
.statement had there been a grain of untruth in it. You
took upon yourself to contradict it "on your own." You
said that the acrobats couldn’t have invisible wires con-
cerned in the feat unless they wanted to cut themselves
into halves, &0., &o. What really occurred at Melb.
Royal was this. The top acrobat, in falling across the
mimic chasm, failed to grip the strap at the opposite
bank. The bridge, therefore, was only supported on one
side of the chasm, and it ought to have swung down against
the “cliff”—but it didn’t. The three men remained
suspended in mid-air by the wire-ropes attached to their
belts (your second letter suggests that you know all about
the arrangement), and the curtain was lowered hastily
to give the Bridge a chance to adjust itself. Now, when
your original contradiction was ridiculed by “ J. T. Mao.,"
you wrote again, denying that any wire or any trickery was
used in the Living Bridge feat. By the wav, Mr. George

Dear Bulletin, —In return for the loss of my scalp, let
me mildly point out to the gratuitously ferocious “ N."
that to require me to vindicate my indictment of
“ ” by “sending along a gross or
two of my own ” is no more reasonable than it would be
to preclude me from calling the three-card-trick a swindle
unless I could perform it myself. With regard, also, to
what is practically “ N.’s ” abuse of the other side,
I should be quite as much justified in predicting,
from “N.’s” approval of Wilde’s work, his approval
also of Wilde’s practices, as “N.” is in insinuating
that my opinion of the productions has been modified
by the disgrace of the producer—that it is an enviously
disingenuous opinion—and that I am necessarily a “small
critic" because I have not the felicity of agreeing with
an inferentially great one. Beyond which, since I have
neither space nor inclination to carry the war properly
into"N.’s” territory, let me simply wish him, with Don
Farrago, “ either a feebler foe, or a better temper!”—M.

pected of an intention to try a dramatic season at Melb.

Nearly every member of Maggie Moore’s Co. lost

Newman, M.L.A., spent “a monkey ” on
the Orange festivities to N.S.W. Premier Reid,
When Mayor Snowden was asked to allow
the Davitt procession to pass down Oollins-street,
Melb., from Spencer-street station, on a Saturday
afternoon, he refused, on the alleged ground that
it might block the tramway at Elizabeth-street
or Swanston-street. He said, however, that the
procession could go down Bourke-street if it liked.
But if a block was possible, it was more likely to
occur in Bourke-street, where there are more
people on a Saturday than in Collins-streefc
—as deserted at 3or 4 p.m. as at midnight.
Yet, a few days after Davitt’s arrival, a procession
of 300 or 400 butchers’ carts was allowed to parade
Collins-street in the middle of an ordinary working
day; and while loaded trams were in full swing.
So much for the impartiality of Mayor Snowden. *
