A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, bub it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this impeitfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion v of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
The Wild-Cat Column.
Ifc is announced that the big firm of Golds-
brough, Mort and Co., Limited, intends applying to its
debenture-holders for a reduction of interest from 5 to
8 per cent. When the oonoern reconstructed in ’93 the
debentures were fixed on the 5 per cent, basis—which
certainly looks like an atrociously high rate ; and as there
■gt ere £2,450,000 worth in circulation the interest repre-
sented £122,500 per annum. The proposed change, if
effected, will save £49,000 a year, which will he avast
relief to the overburdened co. Goldshrough, Mort and
Co., with its £2,450,000 of debentures, £600,000 owing in
Other<directions, and only £403,000 of paid-up'capita), is
obviously top-heavy, and if it had a great deal more local
capital, and a great deal less money borrowed in England,
it would get on much better.
• • • •
If Goldsbrough, Mort and Co., the Gity of Mel-
bourne Bank, and the Australian Deposit and Mortgage
Bank, which are all appealing for a reduction of interest,
carry their reconstruction schemes through, the amount
which Australia has to send to England in the shape of
interest on British capital will be reduced by £lOO,OOO per
annum. Which is one good feature in the case, at all
events.
*•■ * *
The Colonial Bank of Australia got its second
re-construction scheme through almost without a mur-
mur. The reduction of deposit interest to 2J per cent,
was carried by a vast majority; the depositors merely
stipulating that the interest was to be raised to 3 per
cent, if the profits would admit, that they were to nom-
inate three of the directors while the present arrange-
ment lasts, and that no divs. were to be declared until the
depositors were paid. An obtrusive person named Pin-
schof endeavored to distinguish himself by proposing a
rival reconstruction scheme of his own. Mr. P. wanted
to issue “A” mortgage bonds at 5 per cent, interest for
half the existing deposits, and “ B ” mortgage bonds at 4
per cent, for the other half, the “A” bonds to be re-
deemed in a complicated fashion on three separate prin-
ciples. The interest on the “B ” bonds was to he paid if
the bank could manage it, and) failing that, it was to
remain unpaid. Pinsohof went to a considerable expense
in advertising his scheme, which certainly possessed some
advantages, but (everybody not being a Teuton with a
chess-board mind) the depositors couldn’t wrestle with its
various complications and gave it up.
♦ * * *
The City of Melbourne Bank, which commenced
its reconstruction proceedings long before the Colonial,
hasn’t even been heard from lately, and the manager is
still understood to be struggling with the Scotch and
English depositors, and explaining things at great
length. Probably by this time the City of Melbourne
people have begun to fully realise that British capital is
not anything like the thing it is cracked up to be. If the
Colonial had also possessed a great mass of British
depositors the chances are that its reconstruction would
have dragged along wearily for six months or
more, to the misery and exasperation of every-
body concerned. Since the Colonial took its plunge
and got through successfully, it is an open secret
that two or three other hanks think seriously of doing
likewise. The trouble with them, however, is that they
all hold a considerable amount of British deposits and will
have to call meetings in Great Britain and various pro-
vinces of Australia, and apply toquite a number of courts
for sanction, before anything can be done. And at any
one of these various stages an aggravated Judge with a
liver may destroy the whole scheme. Failure in such a
case wou'ld be disastrous and would probably, mean
liquidation, and under these circumstances it is just pos-
sible that they_will try to struggle through on the pre-
sent basisf In that case, the Colonial, with its reduced
rate of interest, should get a long start.
* » * •
Melh. bonded-store keepers are “sick” just
now. Prior to Reid’s budget, the re-imposition of the
N.S.W. tea-duty was feared, and, as a consequence, an
enormous weight of tea was exported to the Harbour
City, emptying the bonds. Now the bond men are nightly
dreaming of the arrival of new season’s stuff (due in
July). At no previous time have the Melb. bonds had
such empty stomachs.
* ♦ * *
Many otherwise unletable flats in boom (city)
structures are now occupied as residences. In many are
to be found parties of youths “ batching,” and in some
families. The ren ts, of course, are low (a few shillings per
week); but what is home without a back-yard?
♦ * * ♦
The Guardian Accident Assurance and Guar-
antee Co. of Australasia, Ltd., has at last gone into
liquidation. Its balance-sheets for some time have
formed amusing reading for experts. Had it not been
for the forbearance naturally shown by the press for so
good an advertiser, the end would have been much
sooner.
* * » •
The price of leather has risen, and there is a
tendency to out bootmakers’ wages. Apropos, some big
strokes have lately been done in hide-deals. On one
£5OOO transaction which went through a Sydney bank
over £3OOO net profit was realised.
• * * •
A lawyer, intimately connected with a high
Vic. personage, has settled his difficulties for 2s. 6d. in
the £.
* * * *
No one can file his schedule in Victoria without
paying full fees ; in forma pauperis is not recognised in
the Insolvency Court. So a man who gives everything to
his creditors before seeking this haven of refuge, cannot
pass the portal until he finds a friend who will lend him
the needful.
* ♦ * *
A certain cheerful Melbournian is said to have
put his back against the wall of his liabilities, and defied
the banks to sell him up.
• » * ♦
One big retail Melb. grocery firm has 49 sub-
urban branch shops.
» * * *
Judge Moles worth (Vio.) has decided that a
lawyer who prepares an insolvent’s schedule must rank
as an ordinary creditor in the estate. One lawyer, claim-
ing £l4, had to be satisfied with 6d. in the £.
♦ * * *
A Bulletin Melb. correspondent : One-man-
two-hillets Chief-Justice Madden, Acting-Governor, has
reserved judgment in a case completed more than a month
ago ;it was certainly lengthy but not complicated. Many
of the concerned are from other provinces, and are im-
patiently waiting here at great expense.

Ed. Bulletin, —Re a recent Bui.irtin para-
graph anent the values of the V’VL. Bank lottery
prizes:—(1) There was no prize of £6OOO value drawn for
in the lottery. There was a prize of £6500, and, on a por-
tion of the property, a first mortgage of £lBOO, which the
mortgagees hesitated to release, as they considered the
security hard to beat. This property, or rather the por-
tion on which the mortgage rested, was valued by the
Govt, assessor at £5500, and the winner has not sold, but
is making arrangements to work. Further, the other
prizes which come nearest to this prize in value are the
first prize of £26,000 and the fourth prize of £l5OO, neither
of which have been sold. (2) With reference to the tenth
Erize valued at £750, and the alleged offer of £l5O, it cannot
e taken as any criterion of value, as three independent
valuators affirmed the value of this property at the price
stated in the lottery list ; and, bad as times are in Tas-
mania, they are not so bad but that considerably more
than 12s. per acre (246 acres) could be realised for a farm
of good land, well situated, close to a railway-station, and
within 30 miles of Hobart. (3) As to the city allotment,
value, £lOO, said to be worth perhaps £2O, this statement
1s rather severely confounded by the fact that the
trustees of the bank have, dnring the liquidation, sold,
lor cash, two adjacent allotments nob one whit better at
£lOO each.—Very truly yours, A Shareholder.

The Manly Co-operative Steam Ferry Co.
(Sydney) announces by advt. in another column a neyv
issue of 15,000 shares of £1 each, of which Is. per share is
payable on application, Is. on allotment, and the balance
by calls of Is. at intervals of not less than a month. The
Co-operative Ferry Co. has proved a splendid investment
up to date, the profits for the last half-year be|ng
equal to 131 per cent, per annum on the exist-
ing small capital. Also, the Co*, had, on 30th
April, £929 accumulated profit on hand, which amount
has since increased, so that one 7 per cent. div. on the
new issue is practically provided in advance. The new
capital is wanted for the purchase or construction of
boats, so as to save the heavy outlay now incurred for
rent of-steamers, and also with anview to providing vessels
of a better class than those at present in use.

Why oan’t some Coolgardie correspondent send
something definite about-the new find in the Londonderry?
People get too much “rich as ever” and too little
measurement. What is the length of the rich ore„ shoot ?
The sinking of-a winze would indicate that. They have
passed it and it is short. Is it above the drive? And, if
so, why don’t they rise on it? The winze is down 10ft.,
and though two or three leet of comparatively poor quartz
was passed through, the bottom is declared to be wonder-
fully rich. If it is as rieh as the surface hole they should
have got at least, 10,000 oz. out of six feet of the winze
and another 10,000 oz. from the drive ; and 20,0000 z.
would boom the shares beyond par. They aren’t above
par just now.

Great Boulder (Hannan’s) —122 tons, 1016 oz.
This mine has turned the table on. tjrmy a man’s predic-
tion. After it was floated in London shares dropped to a
few shillings, and for a time they disappeared from the
Exchange-list. The existence of the present rich ore-body
was then practically unknown. The Bulletin learns
from a well-known mining examiner that the show is
looking remarkably well.

The reef in the 380 ft. level of Bayley’s is declared
to be lift, wide, but correspondents are distinctly silent,
about that rich “bonanza.” It must have cleared out

* * * *
From Coolgardie : “ The blacks around Danns-
ville are very troublesome.” The blacks probably think
the boot is on the other leg. The writer heard one Cool-
gardie man boast that he had killed more W.A. blacks
than they had killed whites altogether, and that he had
burnt eight in one wood-heap. Adding that they burn
best with their legs outward—a very handy thing to know.
* * * *
Coolgardie makes a poor show in crushings as
compared with puffings. The export of, 85650 z. for May is
about equal to the yield of a second-rate Easternfleld,
and falls much below that of first-rank fields like Charters
Towers or Rockhampton. The total Westralian gold-
export; per month, in fact, is less than any one of half-a-
dozen Eastern centres of production. Admitting the
possibilities of the future, is it not time something prac-
tical began to be shown?
The Murchison May yield was 65620 z.; Pilbarra,
27190 z.; Yilgarn, 1288 oz.; and Kimberley, Dundas, and
Ashburton, 710 oz. among them. These figures are, how-
ever, given as those exported only : possibly the actual
yield was considerably greater. Returns of tonnage, by-
the-by, are always lamentably wanting‘from Westralian
newspaper mineral statistics.
• * * *
Some men in W.A., with an eye to big biz, have
offered to build the Coolgardie railway for £61,000-much
less titan half the estimated cost. Of course ttiis tender
has been described in newspapers as absurdly low—a hint
that the contracts should be given to some other firm;
The disappointment of some tenderers can be well im-
agined. With thousands of tons nf machinery goinfr to
the field besides the ordinary traffic, and remembering
what was made by the contractor of the Southern Cross
line before it was taken over, the Coolgardie line offers
every temptation for cheap construction in the quickest
time on record. If the successful tenderers have com-
plied with the preliminary conditions, it is their business
as to whether the price is absurdly low, and they should
get the contract, There are already too many Govt,
scandals in W.A. without adding another here.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
“ Negotiations proceeding for the erection of an
additional 30 head on the Big Day Dawn, Cue, which,
with new winding-plant, will cost £25,000.” Never having
paid a div. with 20 heads, the writer congratulates the old
shareholders on getting rid of the mine.
* • * »
Wilson, of the Investors’ Review, remarks
that, within 14 months, 75 W.A. mines have issued pro-
spectuses in London with a nominal capital of over eight
millions, and that most.of them have no paying prospects,
and three successfully floated have already ignominiously
failed.
• • ♦ *
Squads are leaving Coolgardie daily for Lake
Cowan, the scene of the latest rush, where alluvial has
been found at the base of a big ironstone hill. To await
confirmation of a new rush at Coolgardie is to get there
when it is worked out. The Lake is 65 miles away, and
over 1000 men are there already.
» » * *
“The Central Exploration Co., which bought
Henning’s Black Flag property, is making arrangements
to erect a 100 head stamp battery immediately.” It would
only cost £30,000 to £40,000, and require a million gallons
of water a month.
* * » *
One Westralian claim is known as the Bread-
and-Water, from the manager’s cheerful habitof retailing
those necessaries to his men. His price for water is 6d.
per gallon ; you can get it elsewhere for 4d.~but, some-
how, all the men on that mine prefer to take it at 6d.
They want to be friendly, perhaps.
* * * •
At the Island, Lake Austin, W.A., writes a
Bulletin correspondent, the mining outlook is by no
means roseate. Alluvial nearly worked out. Two English
co.’s practically monopolise the Island. Their combined
staffs would be more than equal to managing Mt. Morgan.
Also, each runs a 10-head mill in a spasmodic sort of a
way. One of them actually keeps going six hours a day.
Carting water over four miles for boilers ! What prospect
divs. with watered slocks, very short and patchy shoots
of gold, and the bulk of the lode stuff very so so?

After being dead for about six years, the Broken
Hill Stock Exchange was resurrected last week. But its
pulse is feeble, and grows feebler.
* » * ♦
Block 14 new western lode proves more disap-
pointing on better acquaintance.
♦ * * *
The British B. H. is raising the wind by a sale
of carbonates, stacked for some time on the surface, to
Block 14. The stuff goes about 22 per cent. lead and 750 z.
silver. A thousand tons have been delivered.
» * * *
Junction (8.H.) will not venture yet on a ship-
ment of concentrates to Europe, but sends a good trial
parcel to Dry Creek. The machine is making a good deal
of slimes, but is keeping the lead above 60 per cent, and
the zino at about Iff per cent.
* * ♦ *
Attacks are being made on the resolution of the
directors of Block 10 to erect big concentrating works,
and the return of the w-eek ended June 13 is being
quoted to show that the experimental plant is losing 40
per cent* of the lead and 60 per cent, of the silver. That
return is not, however, a fair one ; for the ore dealt with
that week was largely stuff stored on the surface for
many months, and reduced by atmospheric action to
powder, producing much slimes, which are not counted
in the ordinary return.
• • * *
Broken Hill people still feel a little chagrined
at the transference of popular attention from the Barrier
to Coolgardie. Wherefore, with respect to Coolgardie’s
export of 85650 z. gold for May, they point out that they
sent away 50 per cent, greater value in lead than that —
£48,000 worth, in fact.
* * * *
The Central sale has now been confirmed, and
the names-of the persons comprising the purchasing
syndicate are still unknown. The general impression is
that it is a Melb. syndicate, and that the only thing
Yankee about it is the sulphide treatment process.
* * * *
A recent and fairly typical week’s work at the
Block 10 concentrators is held up as evidence that the
results attained are not much to enthuse over. It is put
this way :—Crude ore treated, 342 J tons; containing 78
tons lead, 92 tons zine, and 11,3020 z. silver. Value of
constituent metals: lead, £780; zinc, £1104; silver,
£1412; total, £3206. Concentrates recovered,’B2 tons,
containing 41 tons lead and 37930 z. silver. Value of
metals : lead, £4lO ; silver, £475 ; total, £BB5. The value
(unstated) of the slimes sold has to be added to the latter;
nevertheless, not a third of the metal in the crude ore is
saved. A thousand years hence someone will write a
treatise on the incapable metallurgy of the ancient Aus-
tralians.
Very little is heard of the Block 10 slimes save
that they are sold to local smelting-works. The manage*
ment is not altogether proud of the fact that they are
rather plentiful, and that they have assayed very often
quite close to lOOoz. of silver per ton. No wonder they
can be “ sold to local smelting-woiks.’’
* * * *
In pursuance of the B.H. Central’s policy of
making hay while the sun shines, a contract with Dry
Creek for 3000 tons white lead, and a provisional contract
for 3000 tons of sulphides, have been made. These are in
addition to sales to Block 14 and the getting into blast of
the third smelter.
* » * ♦
A great deal of money has been,thrown away at
Broken Hill through the absence of reliable plans and
surveys outside the big mine. Down to, say, three years
ago these things were done in a most slipshod manner,
so that it has been nothing unusual for a manager to un-
expectedly strike an old working, long since mullocked
up, which explored tho v ery ground he was spending
hundreds of pounds in touting. This can be said particu-
larly of the Central and Junction.
» * * *
From the Northern Territory Chin Look is
carrying on operations sivcessfully at Rowley, keeping
his battery running three shifts on pajable stone. Ah
Loy’s batteiy at Brook’s Creek is running continually and
yielding rich returns. Sim Hay is advertising to let
tributes at 10 per cent, on “ several ” of his mining pro-
perties. The Chinese owners of the Tien Shing mine have
netted a little (or big, who knows?) fortune during the
last nine months. The Chows are without doubt running
the Northern Territory.
* ♦ » *
The spiritualistic syndicate is still boring
patiently for coal at St. Kilda (Melh.). The medium’s
seems that the syndicators have pinned their oracle to a
final promise. The coal is situated at a certain number
of hundred feet lower than they have got to thus far, and
ideas of distance have expanded, and the coal-mine lies
deeper in the ground than his original estimate, but it
they mean to go on boring and believing until they strike
•it rich.
• * * *
A correspondent :—Three Gnlgong (N.S.W.)
diggers lately had an exciting few minutes. They had
lowered the first man down the 180 ft. hole first thing in
the morning, and, feeling the rope sag when near the
bottom, they wondered at the cause, but still lowered
away. Not feeling the man get out of the rope (hey
started to haul him back. Imagine their horror when
they saw' they were hauling up a man by the toes. Oanse:
the lowered man when within 30ft. of bottom met foul
air, and fainted, and slipped out of the sling, which
tightened round his insteps. Fact is sometimes stranger
than fiction.—G.P.L.F.

* * * *
Most raining men imagined from the cabled
summary that the English Court of Appeal had knocked
out the cyanide patents; but the full text of the judg-
ment shows that the Court upheld the patent on nearly
every point, and dismissed the appeal with reluctance
because of an error in the provisional specifications. The
Smith African Commission is now taking evidence in
Sydney.

The shafts are worked down to the level of the water,
where the atmosphere has disintegrated the stone and
. made the gold obtainable. B yond this, when the brown
stone changes to mnndic, the shaft is abandoned, even
when running 2oz. and 3oz. to the ton.

The Canadian who has tapped the
Taranaki (M.L.)petroleum the crude oil is worth 15s.
a barrel (40gais.). The oil gives 75 per cent, pure paraffin.
The flow from the present well is from 30 to 40 barrels a
week. Sea-water interrupts the flow. Another bore,
which will overcome the water difficulty, is to he sunk
elsewhere.

The sale of tickets in connection with the
distribution of the famous St. Alban’s estate is still
proceeding apace, and it is not unreasonable to
expect that the drawing will take place during
September. An illustration and “Tattersall’s
(Mr. Georg© Adams’) advertisement, giving neces-
sary business particulars of the monster art-union,
will be found on page 21.
The fall of the Rosebery Ministry is but
in accordance with omens. The suspicion of
Windsor backstairs intrigue about the choice of
a Premier to succeed Gladstone ; the half-hearted
acquiescence of Harcourt, who had better claims
to the leadership; the open discontent of Labou-
chere and the extreme Radicals—these things
made a bad beginning for the clever leader. He
did not improve upon his beginning. His speeches
bristled with offence. The awkward admission
that the consent of “ the predominant partner”
was necessary to Home Rule offended the
Irish; a later allusion to some of his own
party as illiterates was kpenly resented. The
Nonconformist conscience, which roared as gently
as a sucking - dove at the bidding of the
canting Gladstone, fretted uneasily under the
whip of the horse-racing Rosebery. Foes
gathered ; friends grew lukewarm; the Liberal
majority dwindled. Bye-elections went unplea-
santly against the Govt. —the recent loss of Mid-
Norfolk coming only as a significant climax*,to
previous defeats. Rosebery proved physically
unequal to the strain, and his old enemy, insom-
nia, aggravated his troubles. A dissolution was
delayed, in the hope that things would mend ;
but the rent in Ministerial fortunes became every
day more obvious. The defeat of the Govt, by
seven votes in the discussion on the Army esti-
mates might have been resisted ; but the evil day
could only be postponed. So the Govt, resigned—
a general election follows ; and the Tories will
almost certainly return to office.

Personal Items.
The new N.S.W. Governor is—the son of
his father.
It is printed in Melbourne that Aubrey
Beardsley is of Tasmanian birth.
Tasmania also has its medically-qualified
lawyer. Mr. H. B. Mugliston, M.R.0.5., of
Hobart, is also an attorney, and prefers to follow
the legal profession.
Parson-editor Fitchett,
speaking on the question of
Methodist unity : “We are mak-
ing history, and behind our move-
ment is the approval of a great
multitude of people—in fact, it
is approved by all persons out of
lunatic asylums and gaols.” A
painfully ungenerous utterance
from a churchman, considering
the many zealous supporters the
churches have in gaols and lunatic
asylums.
Yic. Chief Justice Madden
knows more than is between the
covers of the law-books. Re-
cently counsel was elaborating an
argument which seemed to imply
that a certain person had been in
two places at once, when Madden,
C.J., interrupted with: “I am
afraid, Mr. Blank, that you are
endeavoring to import the prin-
ciples of esoteric Buddhism into
the law of contracts.” Again,
in acertain marine case, the O.J.
was assuming the position of a
certain line amidships from date
given from fore-and-aft. “ I
would like to know where your
Honor gets that ?” said counsel, to
whom answered the Bench, “I
get it in the first place from the
12th proposition of the first book
of Euclid.”
Chin Young, the oldest and wealthiest
backblocks Chinaman, is about to take a trip to
Chimi. He has been storekeeping in western
N.S.W. for 37 years—nearly 30 of which were
spent at Bourke.
Potts Point, Sydney, was named after a
clerk in the Bank of N.S.W., who served the
institution from its foundation in 1817 until 1842.
He was a good soul, if one may judge from the
inscription on his tomb in the old Sandhills ceme-
tery.

Acting-Gov. Madden belongs to a line of
long livers. His father is a veteran Melbourne
huntsman safely past four score, who still never
misses a meet, while the grandfather of the
dapper Chief Justice regretfully tore himself
away to other spheres after a mundane residence
of 104 years.
The announcement of the marriage of
Boyd Morehead to Miss Seymour has rather
a, tonished his friends in England, writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, as it was
generally supposed among Anglo-Australians that
he sought an alliance not altogether unconnected
with vice-royalty.
Premier Seddon’s eldest daughter is a
school-teacher in the Wellington (M.L.) province.
W. K. Simms, the millionaire Adelaide
brewer, left for Europe early in June to drink the
waters at Carlsbad.
Ex-Premier Shiels, the volatile and bril-
liant father of Victorian Divorce, is now, The
Bulletin regrets to hear, but the wreck of his
former self.

Old David Mitchell, Melba’s father, owns
a hill of limestone at Lilydale, Vic., out of which
he makes stacks of coin. Cave Hill is of the
silurian age, and its component limestone is full
of fossils.

A London paper relates
how once, when Prof. Morris
was lecturing on history at
Melb. University, a R.C.
student rose and denied a
statement made by the Pro-
fessor, and showed as his
authority a passage in a
book by the Professor’s
brother, a Jesuit, who died
lately in London.
A number of Melb.
persons who for long have
patronised Adam Lindsey
Gordon through the columns
of the daily press, and
whose annual snivel over the
grave of the bard has been
regularly recorded, have
assembled in the City of Bad
Odour for the purpose of
forming a Gordon society,
and fostering an interest in
A. L. G. by giving recitals of
his works. And yet poor
Gordon is interesting, in
spite of the remorseless
people who recite “ The Sick
Stockrider.”
Artist- Lionel Cowen,
who leaves Melbourne for
London shortly, has been
fortunate in securing a great
admirer for his work in Mrs.
Silas Harding, Victoria’s
richest widow, who has
bought recently, at substan-
tial figures, several of the
artist’s pictures. Mr. Cowen
married, some years ago, a
remarkably handsome
woman, who has figured in
several of his pictures, and is
the mother of the smallest
infant in Australia. The
child, a boy, is now seven (7)
months old, and measures
only twelve inches, and its
hands and feet are micro-
scopically small. Despite
this, young Cowen, an ob-
ject of great interest to the
medical profession, is per-
■ fectly healthy and thrives
well.
Premier Seddon (M.L.) is to be presented
with a birthday address, and Mrs. Seddon with
1000 sovereigns. Since the enfranchisement of the
Maoriland woman, a Premier’s wife is evidently
a person of large political importance. So far as
The Bulletin knows, this wife-testimonialising
is without precedent in Australasia.
It is surmised {vide last week’s cable) that
“ Pijedatoros ” Robinson will take the temporary
governorship of Newfoundland. Poor old Bill
Robinson ! How can a man of his warm tempera-
menb be expected to compose love ballads on a
stone-broke island, where the normal winter
temperature is 30 below zero, and the inhabitants
eat cod-fish seven days a week ?
Of 18 Vic. Premiers, six allowed a
K.O.M.G. to be tied to their tails —O’Shanassy,
Sladen, M'Culloch, Duffy, Berry and Patterson.
The only Vic. Premier with a hereditary title was
O’Loghlen, Bart., hereditary Prince of Burren,
begorra, who, by the way, completes his 67th
year on June 27, though you wouldn’t think so to
look at him.
J. B. Patterson, K.0.M.G., has just got
out from England the star and ribbon of the order
of knighthood to which he now belongs, and has
been wearing the pretty things round his neck at
a lot of recent banquets in the country. The St.
Michael and St. George colors are red-and-blue—
red, the color of meat, and blue, the color of a
butcher’s smock. Rosebery is a humorist.
E. G. Fitz Gibbon, chairman of Melb.
Board of Works, and poet of gutters and sewers,
is a sensitive flower, and squirms like a hooked
worm under criticism. A sample squirm from
the Argus
For forty years and longer towards whom am I a wronger?
I have all that time been living under open public glare,
And am one that straight defies all the prying hundred
eyes
To find out one unworthiness, and freely lay it bare.
How could Ibe unemployed ? Could I not fill up the void
As cleverly as you can, and mould my own career ?
I am no hireling slavish, nor sordid trickster knavish,
And ’tis for those who know to say if I am paid too dear.
They could have bade me clear.
If they had, in quick compliance I’d have trusted self-
reliance,
Without fear.
Fitz. modestly admits his infallibility, and defies
the world to find in him “one unworthiness.”
Probably the White Knight has never heard of
paranoia—the disease that afflicts the man who
“ goes dotty ” from a prolonged survey of his own
perfections. Verse like the above would in some
people be regarded as one of the very worst
symptoms of paranoia.
R. W. Lewis is one of the
most brilliant, popular, and
positively the most unlucky of
Australasian cyclists. If Lewis
rides in a race, and in that race
a man is destined to fall, or to
be “baulked ” or “ pocketed,”
that fated one will be Lewis.
And his hard luck gave him
the hardest knock of all when
he raced Megson not long ago
in the Five Mile Champion-
ship. All the way round
Lewis led Megson ; through-
out the long five miles Lewis
breasted the wind, shielding
Megson; over every inch of
every lap Lewis made the pace
and bore the worry of it; and,
behind him, peddling ding-
dong after his hind wheel, rode
the wily foe, with his iron jaw
set upon the single task of
hanging on to Lewis. Then
in the last 50 yards Megson
“got the go” on Lewis and
beat him on the post by an
eyebrow. Lewis is a Sydney
man, 25 years old, and has
been a cyclist for three years.
Already he has lowered the
Australian mile cycling record
by 6 1-5 secs., his time being
2min. 8 1-5 secs. And the
other day, when he won the
25-Mile Championship of Vic-
toria, he put up a new record
of Ih. 4min. 53secs. as against
the previous best of Ih. 6min.
47 l-ssec. Lewis is also a
grand footballer ; “ the best
three-quarter-back in N.S.
Wales” according to his
strong following of masculine
and feminine admirers.
Journalist: “ Higinbotham is painted as ■
a kind of amiable sumph in his barristerial ref-
lations. Now, he was defending counsel in a
libel action v. my paper, and the style in which

J. G. Burtt, who for years represented
North Melbourne in Vic. Assembly, is still to be
seen in Collins-st., setat, say, 85. He once beat
George Higinbotham at an election, and years
afterwards, when Burtt’s seat was in danger and
Higinbotham was in the House, the latter went and
addressed a big and noisy meeting on Burtt’s .be-
half. J.G.B. was a henchman of Father Mathew
50 years ago, and attributes his health and
longevity to “ water straigho.”
A Maoriland pioneer has just died at West
Maitland, where he had resided for 50 years.
John Sawyer, who was 80 when he “went over,”
was one of a surveying-party who went to M.L.
in 1835 on behalf of the N.Z. Company. Many
persons, who did not know deceased, believed him
identical with a man of the same name who
figured in Major Mudie’s “ rebellion ” at Castle
Forbes, just beyond Maitland ; but this could not
be, as Mudie’s trouble occurred in the thirties, and
Mr. Sawyer did not reach the Hunter until 1846.
Clark Arthur Irving, barrister-at-law, and
once a N.S.W. Crown Prosecutor, died in Sydney
last week. He was a native of N.S.W., and one
of the few wits of the Australian Bar, but he
■lacked industry and other things often possessed
by duller conversationalists. Clark Irving was a
college-mate of Lord Rosebery, who was glad to
renew their old acquaintance on his Australian
visit, and will regret to hear of the death of a man
who, in his early manhood, was one of the
brightest and most genial of spirits.
Rev. James Bickford, [the oldest Wes-
leyan minister in S. A., and one of the best-known
members of the denomination in Australia,
died last week. Mr. Bickford, who was
everywhere respected as a good and charitable
man, was born on 6th May, 1816, and went asji
missionary to the West Indies in ’3B. He arrived
in Sydney with the late Dr. Kelynaok and Mr.
Ournow (now editor of S.M. Herald), and has
ever since, until his superannuation a few years
ago, worked hard for the church in Australia, He
had been thrice president of the Australian con-
ference.

Melb. University students are starting a
new paper, Alma Mater, which will probably
share the fate of its predecessors, the University
Review, Undergrad and Quadrangle. Even
the Speculum, the medical students’ organ, is
deceased.

There will ere long be another secession
from the Turner Ministry. Sam. Williamson, who
took one of the honorary portfolios after Pratt
and Sargood had retired, is known to be opposed
to the Referendum, and will resign when the Bill
is submitted.
One very solemn day, in 1840, there was
just Is. 6d. in the South Australian Treasury.
But the province had no debts in those days. If
it could get rid of its liabilities now, and start fair
with Is. 6d. in hard cash, it would feel a lot better
than it does.

the Northern line be deflected so as to divert
Rockhampton trade to Townsville, the Central
line should not be deflected so as to capture for
Rockhampton trade which at present goes to Bris-
bane, Why not, indeed ?

cate of land-taxation, adopted the idea that, while
the country had so much land to sell, it was better
first to sell it, and then, after it was sold, to tax
it. This is the identical argument used time and
again by MTlwraith, in Queensland, to justify
land-sales and stave off land-values taxation.

“ Protection was, in Victoria, a synonym
for patriotism ; and so long as certain gentlemen
who supported the importers’ interests sneered at
the efforts of the people to supply their own
wants, so long would Protection be synonymous

The N.S.W. Freetraders are making a
song about the duty of £2 ss. charged to a
Bathurat man on an imported wooden leg pre-
sented to him by public subscription. All The
Bulletin has to say is that it’s a wooden-legged
country that can’t make its own wooden legs.
The Reid (N.S.W.) fiscal system abolishes
all duties save those on spirits, tobacco, and nar-
cotics, and exempts from Income-tax those annu-
ally in receipt of £3OO and under. So that, unless
in Ministerial office, the present crowd of teetotal
M.’sP. would pay absolutely nothing to the
revenue.
S.A. Opposition Leader Downer regards
Premier Reid (N.S.W.) as “ an anti-Federal,” and
Parkes as “theoretically a Federal, but practically
indifferent.” He believes that Reid’s Hobart
Conference of Premiers has “ thrown back Federa-
tion for years by introducing new methods to
cause delay.”
The only members of N.S.W. Leg. Coun-
cil whom neither Henry Parkes nor Dibbs had
any voice in nominating are G. H. Cox, G. Thorn-
ton, J. H. Want, and Dr. Garran. Of the 64
sitting members, Oowper appointed one ; Robert-
son, one; Stuart and Dibbs, eight; Dibbs, 15;
Parkes, 37 ; Reid, two.
Dr. MacLaurin’s impertinent challenge to
the Reid Government to “go to the country”
has never been equalled in N.S.W. politics as an
insolent sample of assumed feudal patronage. Its
only parallel is the supposititious case of the street
gamin, who yells after a bishop—“ Hey, mister
“ Well, my little boy ?” “Pull up yer socks !”
Had N.S.W. Leg. Council been bluntly
honest, the burden of its chant would have been :
“ At present, no matter what we may have said,
or how we may have voted in the past, we will not
have a land-tax of any sort, at any price.’’ When
the tax on land-values was first mooted and no
mention made of exemptions, the Council oozed
with crocodile’s tears for the small allotment-
owner ; when the exemptions were inserted —
“Narrow class taxation,” cried the Council.
If Commissioner Eddy is not coming back
to N. S. Wales, that province might do worse
than secure the services of J. H. Smith, the ex-
Commissioner of S.A. Under his management
the S.A. railways regularly yielded the highest
percentage of profit in Australia. This was partly
owing, no doubt, to the cheapness of the lines, for
each mile of railway has to pay interest on .£7OOO
only, while each mile in N.S.W. has to pay
interest on .£14,000 ; but it was also partly owing
to Smith, who is a brainy 'man and much hated
by the political log-roller.
Dickson, M.L.A. (Q.), is of opinion that
“ women would be better employed looking after
their homes than in exercising the suffrage.”
Exercising the suffrage, in the case of a man, only
means that he has to spend half-an-hour in each
three years voting against people like Dickson.
But, when woman’s suffrage is in question, it is
generally implied that to own a vote makes it

The Maoriland Govt, has a Bill on hand
to largely reduce the number of Local Govern-
ment bodies. The Maorilanders have great faith
in the virtues of local government, but at last they
begin to think that—with Drainage Boards, Har-
bor Boards, River Boards, Road Boards, Town
Boards, School Boards, Hospital and Charitable-
aid Boards, District Boards, County Councils,
Borough Councils, and other Boards, Councils,
Trusts, &e.—a good principle has been much over-
done. Nearly every spot of land is bossed by
four different Boards, and it is proposed to amal-
gamate a lot of these for the sake of economy.
“ A straw shows.” It amuses The Bulle-
TIN to see certain N.S.W. potentates preparing
for, or rather trying to get ahead of, the wrath
that is to come. The inevitable release of George
Dean—inevitable should no new and startling
light be thrown upon the North Shore mystery—

Four new railways are promised in variou ß
parts of N.S. Wales—subject to Parliamentary
approval, which is practically a foregone conclu-
sion. Meanwhile, that comprehensive Betterment
Act, which was to prevent the systematic looting
of the Treasury by landowners under the pretence
of “compensation,” seems as far oft as ever. Also,
there is no sign of any attempt to build the lines—
which are all comparatively cheap ones—out of
revenue, and the same old loan which blooms in
the spring is to be resorted to as usual. There is
a lot of monotony about the ways of Australian
financiers. The chief reason assigned for the new
railways is that they will supply work for about
800 men, and, in view of this, Mr. Young thinks
the “unemployed difficulty” should cease from
troubling for this year, anyhow. As the Labor
Bureau reports 8000 or 10,000 unemployed in the
county of Cumberland alone, The Bulletin
hereby sets down Mr. Young as a sanguine ass.
Among Premier Reid’s promised saving ß
for the year Ist July, ’95, to Ist July,’96, are £I6OO
from the allowance for assistance to old and sick
aborigines ; <£lso from the allowance for food for
prisoners in lock-ups; .£2BOO from the vote for
medical comforts and food for the insane; £I4OO
from the grant for amusements, &c., for the
insane; <£soo from the grant to the Coast Hos-
pital ; .£IOOO from other hospital grants ; .£590
from the allowance for inquests and medical
attendance in case of any sudden outbreak of
disease; and .£2700 from the allowance for the
maintenance of the sick poor in hospitals. Also,
the vote for the Benevolent Asylum is cut down
by £SOO, and that for the maintenance of deserted
children by <£soo ; .£7O is saved off the “ medical
attendance in minor gaols; <£3oo off the Boys’
Home and Reformatory ; and a number of smaller
savings of the same kind are promised. Alto-
gether, 1896 is going to be a cold year in N.S.W.
for the deserted infant and the pauper lunatic and
the old rheumatic aboriginal.
Major-General Tulloch, late of Victoria,
has been good enough to acquaint Hingland with
the true causes of Melbourne’s bank failures.
“State socialism, and the action of professional
politicians, resulting from payment of members ”
are responsible for Victoria’s busted condi-
tion, according to this bigoted man in buckram.
It is true that the loss on the Government rail-
ways is attributable to the jobbery of a paid Par-
liament. So much may be conceded in Tullooh’s
favor. On the other hand, we have the glaring
fact that the leading railway wire-pullers were
men who would abolish payment of members if
they could, whilst the bank breakers to an odd one
were the then “ wealthy ” politicians to whom a
matter of .£3OO salary was of no importance what-
ever. All the evidence bearing on the connection
between Victorian politics and local financial
disasters goes to show that an unpaid House,
elected on the English system, would probably
have brought the country to much greater grief in
a much shorter time. Can Tullcoh mention one
labor member responsible for a sinful railway line
or a bank swindle?
N.S.W. Fafcman (yesterday): “I do not
think the time opportune for a Local Government
Bill; the country districts have not yet cried out
for the power to tax themselves, and it is the duty
of the State to construct and maintain all the
main roads of the province.” Fatman (to-day):
“ I have always opposed a land-tax as being un-
necessary until at least a thorough system of taxa-
tion under local government was provided.”
The Vic. pension-list continues to swell.
Bowing to a Full Court judgment, Govt, has
decided to pay all pensions calculated upon salaries
with the annual value of quarters and allowances
added. So that where a civil servant was blessed
with a house, wood, water, gas, apd pasturage for
his goat, the yearly value of those perks must be
tacked to his pay, and the pension assessed on the
lump sum. This judgment will cost Vic. about
.£IO,OOO a year.
Minister Best
(Vic.), in his Budget
speech, said he could
not always distinguish
a revenue-tax from a
protective-tax. This is
a relic of the old heresy
that protective taxes
should produce re-
venue. Protection has
no more to do with
Customs revenue than
has Freetrade. Protec-
tion is an effort to
divert trade, Freetrade
is an untaxed trade.
The revenue duties in
Pi'emier Reid’s Budget
are a hindrance to
trades that cannot be
made local —they are to
Protection and Free-
trade what the mule is
to the horse and the
ass.

Grimes, Queensland M.P., held a meeting
recently at Ipswich, and “ severely criticised the
Labor-party, which he accused of disloyalty
to the Queen.” Grimes then asked the audi-
ence to sing the Unnatural Anthem, and
half the crowd joined in, the other half ex-
pressing dissent by hooting and pounding with
its feet. Finally Grimes recited the Anthem
all by himself, and gave three cheers for Her
Gracious. It would be interesting to know if
Grimes’ loyalty ever led him to sacrifice anything,
even two coppers, for his beloved Queen, or if he
ever got his feet wet on her behalf, or helped her
in any way. Supposing the Queen should be
dethroned by a revolution, and she asked Grimes
to take up arms on her behalf and go on long night
marches with a rifle on his shoulder—what would
be the exact value of Grimes in such a contingency
as that ?
Mr. Arthur Griffith, M.L.A. (N.S.W.)
writes as follows : —
Dear Bulletin, —You say “ the fiscal attitude of the
N.S.W. Labor-party is incomprehensible.” As a matter
of fact, the N.S.W. Labor-party hasn’t got a “fiscal
attitude ” at all, and was elected on the distinct under-
standing that it would not assume one. It wants a land-
tax, a State bank, and several other things, and will not
support any Govt. (Freetrade or Protectionist) that will
not give them. It is swallowing the “ tariff reform ” of the
present Govt, for the sake of the Land-tax.
To which The Bulletin responds that, whether
it will or not, the N.S.W. Labor-party has a fiscal
attitude. It assumed a Protectionist fiscal attitude
in return for manhood suffrage; it now assumes
a Freetrade attitude in consideration of getting
a Land-tax. Its position in these matters is per-
fectly honest—but, so long as it votes on fiscal
questions, no matter which way it votes, or why it
votes that way or any other way, it has a fiscal
attitude. The N.S.W. Labor-party has never
“sunk the fiscal issue,” for the sufficient reason
that any such sinking was wholly impossible. It
assumes its fiscal attitude from reasons of its own,
and the other parties also assume their attitude
from reasons of their own. They are a different
set of reasons, but the attitude is all there.
With the beginning of the really cold
weather in Melbourne it is strange to see the lead-
ing drapery houses sacrificing their winter goods
to make room for an early show of Spring fashions.
Such is the case, however ; and it is marvellous
this winter to note the extreme manner in which
the prices of articles which one would have
thought would just be in the greatest demand —
such as furs, sealskin jackets, winter mantles,
dress stuffs, &c. —are reduced at Messrs. Hicks,
Atkinson & Sons’ establishment in Collins-street,
during their existing stock-taking sale.
AUSTRALIA, A.D.?
Out of the mist she springs—
The mist of Time so long around her ;
No blood upon her garment clings,
No war has crowned her.
She is not born of strife—
No mothers mourn ; to seal her birth
No martyr yielded up his life ;
But joy and mirth
Her sponsors are ; and Peace
And Plenty, too, are there to clasp her hands,
As on the threshold of her life
The New Republic stands.
Melb.

Public suspicion formulates a theory that
* N.S.W, conspiracy case is being hushed up
officially, or that, at least, attempts are being
made in that direction.
# * *
South Australia maintains its reputation.
Recently the Adelaide Register’s London corres-
pondent gravely informed a wondering province
that Governor-designate Buxton had offered up
“a magnificent extempore prayer” at an Exeter
Hall gathering. This month Lieut.-Gov. Way
left Adelaide for the express purpose of presiding
at a Melb. Methodist Union meeting.
* » ♦
Parson-editor Fitchett, speaking at thebig
Melbourne Methodist meeting last week, could
not leave out the Wesleyan “King Charles’s
Head ” —“ the unspeakable Papist,” to wit. And
thence was evolved a somewhat entertaining situa-
tion. Referring to the Pope’s message of re-union,
he said : “ Before we can obey the Pope’s call and
return to his fold, how many things we know to
be true must we believe to be lies, and how
many things we believe to be lies must we
have to take to be true ?” Acting-Gov. Madden,
who had been inveigled to the gathering, visibly
squirmed, and when it came to his turn to make a
few remarks, he quietly said, “ I am not a
Methodist, and the lies which appal Mr. Fitchett
are the truths which sustain me.” And then the
maladroit Fitchett squirmed, while the assem-
bled Methodists shuddered at the discovery—new
to most, as Madden, C.J., keeps his religion
qujet—that an unholy Roman Catholic had been
allowed to jump the billet of Chief Justice and
Acting-Governor.
• • *
Melbournian : “ Why should Chief Justice
Madden be hauled into the Methodist Union
meeting? This is where the religionists make
their big mistake in kootooing to rank and posi-
tion, ever hanging on to the skirts of the rich,
who, of course, need no salvation, while the poor
ber-ruther is damned if he goes to the Caulfield
races programmed this week for excellent
Madden.”
• * •
The unworldly of Sydney were absolutely
astonished last week, when city Mayor Lees, a
boss teetotaller and Sunday-school superintendent,
invited the Gaiety Girls to the Town Hall, where
he provided them with music and champagne.
After all, saints are but mortal. Had Mayor
Lees not been born into Sunday-school circles he
would have made a more than passable johnny.

notice, whisky and opoponax .”
JONES: “ Yes, but that’s better than what it used to be —brandy and patchouli.”
THE OLD ’UN : “Ah, the country was never so prosperous as in the good old days, when
it used to be rum-and-onions.”
A petition to have the local postmistress
removed was set afoot in a Sydney suburb, and a
paid canvasser employed by the promoters to
obtain signatures. He conceived the idea of
attending the busy trams leaving that suburb for
the city at certain hours each morning, and
returning suburbanwards at certain hours each
evening. The other morning, the tram-goers
were suddenly cheered by the sight of a scuffle
between two men—a knock-down blow and a
rending of manuscript; a fierce rejoinder ; acatch-
as-catch can and a roll under-and-over all around
the footpath ; clinch ; clear-away ; pm ; bang ;
stouch —and away moved the locomotive. ihe
canvasser had presented the petition to the lady s
husband for ,
It was pretty cool on the part of the secre-
tary of Hobart Art Gallery to write to the agent
for the winner of the first prize in the V.D.L.
lottery, asking him if he felt inclined to present a
work of art to the institution. The reason for
this appears to be that certain Tasmanians had
announced (!) their intention of giving such a
memento if they were fortunate enough to secure
first prize!
* # *
A Collins-street (Melb.) shopkeeper for
some time past has been showing in his shop-
window a number of cabinet photos, taken by a
new process. The collection failed to attract much
attention until a brilliant idea struck the exhibi-
tor, who added a photo, of a woman in the act of
putting on her petticoat. Now, there is a Gaiety
crush in front of that shop.
« * #
The experienced theatrical manager who,
in advance, described Blanche Massey as, perhaps,
the most beautiful woman he had ever seen, has
somewhat puzzled his friends now that they have
seen the dreadfully cold and inanimate Miss
Massey aforesaid, for nobody ever suspected that
manager of admiring nun-like icy beauty—and it
is generally not hard to judge the direction in
which a man’s tastes go. For instance, now that
people have seen several of Julian Salomons’
woman-pictures, on loan in the galleries, they
have made up their minds that the energetic little
Q.O. dreams of the sort of ladies you see on
chocolate-boxes.
» # •
Barcaldine (Q.) lately had a matrimonial
sensation. At a very largely-attended wedding,
the bridegroom astounded those present by reply-
ing to the parson’s question, “Will you have
this woman?” “ I will not.” Then, putting on
his castor, he hastily left the church. Which
recalls a Vic. incident where the lady was
the objector. “Will you take this man,” &0.,
benignly asked the parson. “I will not,” sturdily
answered the lady. “Then what brought you
here?’’ enquired the astonished shepherd. “ Just
to tell him* so,” answered the fair one, turning on
her heel and leaving the church.

Judge Hood (Vic.) announces a series of
dinner-parties to the various professions. The first
will be given to the lawyers ; the next to the
medicoes—and so on. It is necessary to keep the
professions separate, as a fight to a finish between
O’Hara and Purves might represent some damaged
furniture.

Indecenfc-assaults-on-children was the sub-
ject of discussion at a recent “ Pleasant Sunday-
afternoon ” service at Wesley Church, Melbourne.
“ Pleasant ” Sunday afternoon ! Our oath.
* * *
Countess VYachtmeister, in a theosophical
lecture, told a Melb. audience: “Those who keep
pet animals absorb some of the characteristics of
the animals. A lady she once knew, and who
kept a number of cats, developed the faculty
of seeing in the dark, and a cat-like disposition.”
Let us see now, who is the N.S.W. statesman that
keeps the pet monkey ?
• # *
Coroner Youl’s verdict—“ Saturday-night
suffocation,” should be referred to Judge Win-
deyer for report.
Salomons, Q.C., in addressing the Dean
Commission, was very severe upon popular opinion,
citing the case of Archer as an example of its
fallibility. But the learned Q.C. carefully disre-
garded the fact that it was not mere belief in
Dean’s innocence that caused the clamor for a
Commission. The question of Dean’s innocence
was, and is, in fact, quite beside the question.
Popular opinion, in this case, is founded upon
something tangible. It is founded upon the
knowledge that Dean did not get a fair trial : that
the Judge made himself appear unduly anxious
for a conviction : that he first bullied and then
cajoled the jury : and, finally, that the jurors,
through weariness and the railings of an impatient
Judge, were prevented from giving their own
proper consideration to a matter which it was for
them, and for them only, to decide.
Miss MAUD HOBSON.
Photo. by Talma, Melb.
The Dean Commission was not appointed
to try anybody, but simply to find out, by careful
inquiry, whether the Crown hadn’t made a hor-
rible botch of the administration of justice in this
particular case. The Crown had to appoint this
Commission to inquire into itself, because it
became apparent to everybody that it had botched
things badly, and it became necessary, in order to
preserve any semblance of fairness, to make an
investigation and find out whether the botch was
only as bad as it seemed, or a great deal worse.
Under these circumstances, the Crown, seeing
that it is investigating its own blunders, ought, as
indicated by Mr. Chanter, M.P., to pay all costs.
It can’t conduct a fair and impartial inquiry into
its own injustice and stupidity while it pays a
brilliant lawyer 50 guineas a day to prove one side
of the case, and leaves the other side to be sup-
ported by private charity.
» # #
A performance was given at Sydney Royal
last week in aid of the Dean defence fund, and
S.M. Herald makes a remark thereon in a sur-
prised tone :
One feature of the entertainment that must have
forced itself upon the thinking portion of the audience
was the well-to-do air of the people that filled the house ;
and this, it may be added, showed that the Dean defence
movement is not confined to the class of people who
would be regarded as the immediate associates of Dean.
The Herald, which is snobbish to its inmost soul,
evidently has hard-and-fast ideas as to the sort of
people that a man like Dean ought to associate
with. And as the people who filled the Royal
appeared to be “ well-to-do,” it assumes that they
couldn’t be regarded as Dean's associates. There-
fore, it notes, with a certain lingering surprise,
that they, though of an altogether different class,
actually sympathised with a man belonging to the
“ lowah ordahs,” whereas they might have been
expected to let him hang with absolute indiffer-
ence. The Herald’s feelings on this point are
suggestive of the wild astonishment of that other
elderly female who found that Wales ate straw-
berries just the same way as any other man.
• * •
It cannot be seriously contended that
fortune-telling is a result of secular education, as
attempted by Salomons, Q.C., in his special
pleading for the Crown before the Dean Commis-
sion. Secular education is a besom that sweeps
into the dustbin of past absurdities all such
absurdities as astrology, divination, fortune-tell-
ing, witchcraft, &c., &c. Religious education—
that is to say, in the accepted sense of “ religion,”
positively encourages fortune-telling. The Bible
reeks with instances of persons who ‘‘ enquired of
the Lord ” as to their coming fortune in this event
or that. Besides, in the case of Mrs. Dean, did
not Mr. Salomons himself bring forward a person
to prove that she had been a Sunday-scholar ?
* * *
A Melbourne parson remarks that it is “a
solemn mockery to keep the Bible out of State
schools, and afterwards, when the boys and girls
get into prison, give it to them in their cells. ”
Score one to the reverend gentleman. The Bible
ought not to be thrust on prisoners. For instruc-
tion in plain moral philosophy, which is what
criminals chiefly require, the Bible is a useless, or
worse than useless, volume. But what good might
not a chosen secular worK accomplish ! A book
of eloquent essays upon the practical virtues of
charity, honesty, industry, study in literature and
art, self-control, temperance in all things, and
healthy recreation. Imagine such a work, in place
of the ramshackle, contradictory, dry, historical,
mythical, hysterical, frequently indecent, and
utterly humorless Word ! Imagine a parson
preaching from the new Scriptures: “ Deah
friends, on page 302 you will find that Lord Bacon
saith of knowledge that ‘it taketh away vain ad-
miration of anything, which is the root of all
weakness.’ ” A beautiful text for those members
of the congregation who might be addicted to
booking their theatre-seats right next to the
Governor’s box.

Dear Bulletin, —Be the bumptiousness of
Sydney Botanical Gardens officials. Not long
since I was sitting on the grass with my hands
clasping my knees—a posture, by the way, hard
to beat for general comfort—when an official came
up and ordered me to change my position on pain
of prosecution for indecency ! I may remark that
my clothes were in a perfect state of repair.—
PONTE.
t • •
“Melb. Y.M.C.A.,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “has developed a new religioi 3
horror in the shape of secret meetings. At one of
these there were present about 150 young men.
The lights having been turned low (though there
were no sisters present), ‘We will now engage in
silent prayer,’ said the chairman, and instantly
there was a loud thump as 300 kneecaps struck
the floor. Soon, however, the solemn silence was
completely ‘ bust ’ by some barbarous unbeliever
yelling out, ‘ Damn you, don’t pinch me ! ’ A
responsive giggle caused thechairman to turn on the
lights, and there was a hurried demand to throw
the curser out. He went out quick, but not till
he had somewhat mangled the four hysterical
pleaders who had given him away. Then followed
the reading of letters from absent brothers. They
were chock-full of Scripture and remarks to the
effect that the writers’ souls were saved—halle-
lujah ! Again the lights went down, and the
dusky pantomime loomed up in earnest. In
quick succession young men rose to beseech the
crowd to pray for erring sisters and mothers ; also
for their beery fathers, thieving brothers, and
crooked friends. The lights again, a hymn, and
then they poured out into the street. When
details of the follies of girls, parents, and friends
are bandied, it is no wonder that these ‘ secret ’
meetings are popular with the alleged virtuous
young man.” #
Dear Bulletin, —This “Reunion of
Christendom ” gag can only mean what it says on
the assumption that non-Anglican Protestants are
but paynim dogs. Otherwise, it is hard to see
how Protestantism—essentially a negation of
Romanism —can be much affected by the fact that
a subsection of one of its subsections is ready to
back down on that negation. How, in the name
of everlasting bosh, will Christendom be any
“ One ”-er when the Anglican half-Romanist has
become the complete article ? — PAGAN

A divorce-suit, in which a gallant captain
is concerned, promises to provide Melbourne with
toothsome details at the Court’s next sitting. The
petitioner’s narrative is very hot and peppery.
• * *
In the most recently organised Southern
divorce-case the lady alleges that her husband, a
clever medico, comforted himself with another
while she was away on a year’s holiday; and
people are saying that, if he did, he was a very
human sawbones. Such now is the depravity of
Australian society.

A pretty Melb. girl, who seems to have
been rather cruelly treated by a blue-blooded aide,
jias at last consented to console herself with an
ordinary, every-day business-man. They are to
be married shortly.

Sydney policemen are unenviably notorious
for “dealing it out” to prisoners in the watch-
house. More than one alleged case of the kind is
now under the notice of the authorities. Prisoners
would much less frequently be “ dealt with ” in
the lock-up if the recording officer at each watch-
house were an inspector.
* * *
Auckland, M.L., has had a sensation. A
post-office inspector had died suddenly, and a
post-mortem examination of him followed. Dur-
ing the course of this, the coroner, Dr. Philson,
ordered the dead man’s heart to be brought into
the jury-room because “ it would, be an interesting
sight for them to see.” This is regarded as a
shocking and outrageous thing by Maoriland
generally, Especially by the women. It recalls
how a certain surgeon, after attending his old
friend on his death-bed, took out the deceased’s
heart and held it up before the anatomy-class.
“ Ah, my friends,” said the old man, mournfully
tapping the organ with his knife, “ it is lime that
kills us all.”

Butchers, bakers and milkmen throng around
your door for a fortnight. Unclean Chinamen mix
lies with their yellow teeth as they poetize on
welly good weg’tables. Insurance-canvassers
track you by means of the marriage advt., and
offer fabulous advantages in furniture-insurance
and- life-insurance. Strange men hob-nob with
you in the train to offer candles for your kitchen
fat. Widowers and advanced-thought microbes
write you affectionate letters giving full particulars

E/D.Ji :Tfc is more than time that medical
charges, were kept within reasonable bounds. The
• rapacitybf physiciansand surgeonsgrows with their
incomes in too many cases ; high fees- mean high
living, and high living entails a .tendency to haste
and carelesshessin professional work. If medical
; men became mqre valuable to humanity as their
. costliness increased;—if the enlargement of their
household expenditure improved the prospects of
their patients, these fashionable extortioners
would' sfeein entitled to all they can get. The
truth of the matter is just the other way round.
- The ruling passion with the average successful
' practitioner is to cut a dash before the world,
which is very bad for the healing art and the
, public pocket. There are exceptions, of course,
but not many. A leech, or sawbones, is seldom
found achieving much celebrity without the
assistance of an arrogant brass plate, a formidable
staff of servants, and'other vain display. Fools
encourage these things, but that is no reason why
the law, being framed considerably for the protec-
tion of fools, shouldn’t safeguard them against
if. their medical adviser.
E.F. : “ Hospital nurses are prompted to
give extra sympathetic attention to the most
wretched patients by their womanly indignation
at the distinction which the male medical staff
makes in favor of the well-to do. The heart of the
hospital nurse beats in the lady doctor, let us re-
member. Twenty years hence there will be no
men doctors in poor neighborhoods, no “ provi-
dent dispensary ” or lodge practice left for them.
The working classes will by that time have dis-
covered that a duly-qualified woman is likely to
give them a lob more consideration in the back
room, or out of it, than her brother ever gave
for the money.” Perhaps. The Bulletin has
noticed that the nice naval officer gets at least as
much attention from the hospital nurses as the
coarse-handed, shattered laborer.

The libel-action of Allison Smith v. The
Age has already maundered along for three weeks
in the Melbourne first Civil Court, but the
surrounding world pays no heed to it. Chief-
Justice Madden and a jury of six are listening to
the dull echo of this bygone railway rumpus, and
Smith is conducting his own case. People about
the court remark that the ex-Locomotive Super-
intendent doesn’t shine as a cross-examiner. Else-
where nothing in connection with the case is re-
marked at all. Victoria was weary of Allison
Smith and his strained relations with engine-
drivers, greasers, &0., long before he left the service,
and for years past the sight of his name in a news-
paper has warned readers to pass on to the next
subject. If this dreadful libel-suit were to sud-

A Sydney Chinese labor-office announces
that it is prepared to supply Chinese house-
servants, cooks, station-hands, etc., in any number
at fixed rates. Japanese ditto—ditto.
• * *
News of the efforts of a certain West Yic.
minister to induce the local Chows to attend
church reached the ears of some of the local
“ boys ’’—who went and told the Chinamen that
they would get half-a-crown every time they
turned up to worship. On the following Sunday
the Johns all mustered to a man—which so pleased
the good pastor that he gave them a Bible each.
The yellow men reckoned that this was their first
half-crown, so they all attended again next Sun-
day. Bub no half-crown came. This they stood
for a month—and then, finding that there was no
money in the case, and that they had been sold,
they rushed home and jumped upon their Bibles.
There are now in China nearly 40 missionaries
from Australia, men and women !
• * »
A N.S. W. country M.P., noted for the
unanswerable logic of his conclusions, the other
day made a startling discovery. Hearing the
name of a blushing young witness, he asked if
she was the daughter of a certain Mrs. Blank.
An affirmative reply seemed to puzzle his worship,
and for a few minutes he was lost in thought.
Bub the premises being granted, the old warrior
unflinchingly sought the conclusion, and to the
delight of an expectant audience he blurted out :
“Then Mrs. Blank must be your mother !”
* * *
A mystery : In the Albury (N.S.W.)
ranges was lately found a suicide, with his throat
cut, and in one of his pockets a bottle containing
whisky!

The Police Christian Association Institute
is thriving well in Melbourne. One of its objects
is to provide pleasant rooms for the constables to
sib in. A worthy object. Sleeping out o' nights
in draughty doorways accounts for 50 per cent, of
the mortality in the “ foorce.” If the rooms are
aisy an’ convaneyant to a pub., the Melbourne
police may yet be saved.
* * •
Retrenchment has gone so far in the Yic.
Govt, service that policemen’s gloves have been
abolished. Formerly a Vic. policeman would be
fined half-a-crown for being found with his gloves
off, except when tackling a “tough customer.”
Retrenchment has also reached the members’ and
reporters’ lavatories at Parliament House, and has
taken a curious form. In taking the same form
at the “Sootchesb” bank’s Sydney office it has
evolved from a junior clerk a memorable witticism.
Port Melbourne councillor enlarging upon
the necessity of Brassey landing on the local pier ;
“Look at the business the publicans will do!”
That’s about the size of it—each Governor is
mainly an accessory to beer-drinking.
* * #
At Sydney G.P.O. the sign “Registry
Office” directs people to the letter-registration
department. Many unemployed domestics en-
quire there for vacant “ places.”

“If Mrs. Threlfall’s little volume 'con-
tained nothing but the charming verses, ‘To
O.R.F.T.’ (her six months’-old baby), it would be
well worth buying for the sake of them alone.
But several more of her ‘ Starlight Songs ’ are
equally good, with a freshness and sweetness that
call for no slight commendation in these ‘de-
generate’ days.”—Lond. National Observer,
20/4/’95.


Hygiene teaches that nothing is so bene-
ficial to the human constitution as thorough
cleanliness, a theory verified by modern revela-
tions of the deadly microbes which infest the
flesh and blood—especially if we do not keep
ourselves in a clean condition, which ought to be
the first aim of every individual with any esteem
for himself. The use of Amykos has proved to be
very important to this end, by its great sanitary
power over the skin, mouth, teeth, &c. If you
value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration.
