846 Maequarie-atreet North, Sydney,
June 25, 1895.
My Dear Moobabxnda—
The only, freezing circumstance I remarked in
the appointment of Viscount Hampden to the
Cornstalkian Governorship was that such an
“ominous silence ” re Lady H.’s qualifications
was observed in the newspaper details. As a
rule, we are eagerly assured from the very start
that Lady Blank is an amiable and delightful
personage, but this conventional marmalade was
conspicuously lacking in the first burst of Hamp-
den statistics. However, as Lord Hampden (who
has been twice married) is now the proud possessor
of a Duke’s grand-daughter, it is quite safe to take
all the usual compliments for granted, and Potts
Point may congratulate itself on the main issue
without regard to personal peculiarities. Sydney
society will stand a lot of one-fingered hand-shak-
ing and “indisposition ” if it may talk about what
“ the Duke of Devonshire’s grand-daughter said
to me the other di.”
The Viscount is decidedly a family man. Of
his five sons, the eldest, the hon. “Tom ” Brand,
is a lieutenant in the 10th Hussars, which are
Tummy’s very own warriors, I believe. In
between him and Geoffrey, set. 10, are Hubert—a
naval lieutenant on board the Orescent—Robert
and Roger (beardless youths of 17 and 15);
Margaret, who is 25, Alice (20), and Dorothy (18)
—ladies whose family dates back to “the con-
quest.” Solemnly regarding the Viscount as he
roosts among the branches of his spreading family-
tree, it really looks as if, in him, N.S. W. is about
to hold the long-distance pedigree record for Aus-
tralia —now that Carbine has left the country.
I have a vague feeling that Gov. Hampden will
be a success, and really there was never in this
world a softer vice-regal “snap ” than to follow
in the foot-prints of poor Governor Duff. His
late Excellency has left no giddy heights of suc-
cess to be scaled by his successor—no memories of
prodigal social entertainments to be lived up to by
the new-comer. Even a moderately genial, soci-
able and tactful society man should be able to
take Potts Point by storm.
Talking about Governors reminds me that it
isn’t a case of out-of-sight-out-of-mind with all of
them when they doff the cocked-hat of office and
go back ’Ome again. A young lady who got
married at Parramatta six months, or so, ago was
gratified to receive a letter from Lord Jersey, who
had read the notice of the wedding and hastened
to congratulate her 1 This sounds more like Car-
ington than Jersey. And, apropos of the former,
a well-known ’Stralian actress relates that when
she called upon the baron at ’Omeshe was received
by him in the library, which was pervaded by a
cockatoo using the most unparliamentary language
at the top of its voice. “I hope you don’t mind
him, do you?” said the ex-vice-royalty, who was
basking in the bird’s profanity, “ I rather like it.”
No wonder the Oaringtons put off a party upon
the demise of a certain N.S.W. politician of free-
and-easy speech.
“The Buttercup Spinsters” gave a most suc-
cessful ball at Need’s rooms on 18th inst. The
decorations were most artistic, and the happy idea
of having the time for each dance printed on the
programme, so that one could fit in one’s dance
with one’s hour of departure with the greatest
nicety, is a novelty I sincerely hope to meet again
during the hop season. Buttercup color was, of
course, predominant in the supper decorations,
and Mr. Hardy’s music was right up to date.
Add to all these ingredients a crowd of well-
dressed “ dancing girls,” and it can be easily
understood that the masculine interest was as two
to one, and that the wall-flowers of at least that
evening wore dress-coats, ■ •
Some of the frocks were very neat. Miss Nellie
Baass (one of the hon. secs.) wore a well-cut pink
silk and a pouch-front bodice of pale-
blue and pink brocade with a mock fastening of
diamond studs—an exceedingly stylish gown.
Miss Ella Sparke (her coadjutress) was in pale-
blue corded silk dandified i with gold passemen-
terie, and Miss Sparks wore pink silk with white
lace at all points. A very pretty dark girl wore
white brocade with success, but a short lady in
white silk with big puff ball-sleeves (which did
not efface the nocturnal impression, for sleeping-
suits are cut very saucy just now) seemed to be out
taking the edge off her new robe de nuit.
I have it from a fatally fascinating youth, who
suffers greatly from a rush of pink notes to his
daily mail, that what is called “the Carpet-Pin
Club” has been started in Sydney and, I suppose,
Melbourne too. Its rules and regulations are
mostly unwritten, but its badge is a carpet-pin
worn in a conspicuous position. Girls and boys
(using the word in a “ Gaiety Girl ” sense) are on
the club-lists, and the idea is that when a girl
with a carpet-pin prominently displayed meets a
youth with a similar adornment she and he may
strike up acquaintance and flirt without let or
hindrance even into the jaws of the Divorce
Court. The Carpet-Pin Club is merely an un-
licensed institution for the encouragement of
wholesale flirtation and the only formality in-
separable from joining its mysterious ranks is the
purchase of the pin.
The Dacres got a hearty reception at Sydney
Her Majesty’s last Saturday in that amusing,
smartly-written comedy, “A Scrap of Paper.”
As it was a wet, drizzling sort of evening, there
wasn’t much display in the d.c., where the only
remarkable thing was the extraordinary coiffure
of a lady whose conspicuous locks were arranged
in what an anatomist would call the bowel-twist.
But I liked Mrs. Dacre’s coiffure a la Parisienne,
set off in the first and second acts by a Boulevard
hat trimmed with vivid magenta and green, and
the frock of pale-bluish brocade with small pink
flowers and up-to-date garnitures of magenta
velvet and tinted lace. By the way, seeing how
Mrs. D.’s dresses stood out at the back (and so
do Maud Hobson’s in the “Gaiety Girl”), the
rumor which comes from Melbourne re crinoline
steels in the backs of the smartest frocks would
seem to be true, though in Sydney I only hear of
things like fencing-masks worn on the upper arm
to keep the big, bull-frog-shaped sleeves from col-
lapsing. Apropos, I am sure Maud Hobson must
wear the sleeve-improver, or else a mere hen-like
perk and flutter would never be enough to set her
to rights after Monkhouse has been hugging her
to the visible squashing of her abnormally-big
sleeves. Memo. : Leg-o’-beef-sleeved women who
anticipate being hugged should certainly buy the
improver. And as they know not the day nor the
hour, they should buy it, anyhow.
All this en parenthhse. In the first act Miss
Knights, with her hair becomingly dressed, wore a
graceful gown of pale grey with white points and
a bunch of violets, but I had no patience with
Miss Claudius’s dreadful black riding-habit. In
a day when every well-regulated equestrienne
goes a-gee-gee in a coat, vest, and dickey, the
appearance of a horsewoman in a flowing, bunchy
garment, such as Queen Victoria used to wear in her
horsey salad days, sent the comedy back nearly a
century. It’s an odd thing that nearly every
actress is weak where riding is concerned. I have
seen a good many stage riding-habits, but the only
complete turn-out I ever saw behind the footlights
was worn by a Sydney society woman in a purely
amateur show.
Another thing I objected to in the comedy was
the way Agnes Knights threw the contents of the
Colonel’s writing-table about, in the second act,
while hunting for the scrap of paper. It is not
true to feminine nature for a woman to be so un-
tidy. I don’t say she wouldn’t fling things to the
four winds in a search, but it’s only one woman m
a hundred who wouldn’t carefully put everything
back afterwards. Disorder is immoral —hence
women, who are necessarily more moral than men,
are orderly and neat. Also, the effect of disorder
betrays an upsetting cause, and hypocritical
women always covers up her traces whenever she
can. The last act of the amusing comedy is
smartly dressed by Mrs. Dacre in salmon-pink
brocade, with gold embroideries and pink chiffon;
and Miss Agnes Knights was effective in saffron-
color brocade, with tea-colored lace and a heap of
crimson roses and leaves on the bodice. Mr.
Dacre made an airy, dashing Colonel, and the
mounting was excellent, the Museum-scene being
as full of genuine curios as the Women’s Loan
Exhibition.
On dit that a split is imminent in an Australian
ladies’ club (not to be too precise) over the ques-
tion of the new President. A titled lady
has been absolutely hauled to the position and
given a welcoming tea-party to sign and seal her
Pickwickian appointment; still, as only 25 out of
nearly a hundred members subscribed to the
razzle-dazzle, and as the others stood out in token
of their disapproval of the committee’s action in
foisting a new president on them without asking
their opinion—l really don’t think that welcom-
ing feat can be regarded seriously. The titled
lady in question is not a bit popular with the com-
pact majority of clubsteresses, and unless the
committee make hay of the rules and reg’lations,
and elect her without the usual formality of a poll,
it is more than probable that the ballot-box will
show that the members decline to have even Lady
Blank rammed down their throats in the presiden-
tial capacity. So there, now !
Nellie Stewart’s generous action in handing
over the proceeds of her ben. (.£200) to the poor
little children of Sydney seems to have given a
fillip to infant philanthropy in these parts, for I
see that a Parramatta lady has made a good sug-
gestion to aid the destitute youngsters. One
hundred women are to be buttonholed to subscribe
one shilling per month, and each one so subscrib-
ing is to induce ten others to do so, too. By this
means a goodly sum ought to be got together.
The scheme also embraces an exhibition of
children’s dancing on 17th prox., to be under the
patronage of the acting-vice-royalties. Apropos
of charities, the Woman’s Voice printed this par.
in a recent issue ; “ More than enough to buy food
for a week for a family paid for one seat for one
evening to see the ‘Gaiety Girl,’ while hundreds
of our fellow-women cannot get enough to buy a
meal.” This is all very well, but man does not
live by bread alone. The world must still have its
cakes and ale. The amusement afforded by the
Gee-Gees, a hard-working lot of people, recollect,
does something towards recuperating their
patrons’ energies for the daily struggle. And if
actors and actresses do not live in an atmosphere
of large sums of money, still they are a genuinely
benevolent tribe —as witness the initial lines of this
present paragraph.
Which reminds me that the attendance at the
first Gaiety matinee, at Sydney Lyceum, was
affected by the George Dean defence-fund matinee,
at Sydney Royal, but the play, or whatever-you-
like-to-eall-it, went merrily enough. Blanche
Massey’s love-scenes with “Sharley ” were touched
lightly, and Ryley cut his songs, but considering
the reduced prices nobody expected the complete
night-bill. The gowning was the same, which is
all the matinee woman stipulates for, and except
that Blanche Massey wore a dark-blue dress, with
a bunch of blue flowers, in the last act, which was
infinitely more sensible than the white frock,
there was really no difference in the togging—
though, now I think of it, Maud Hobson wore a
new hat in the first scene. Daylight reveals that
Maud’s gorgeous satin robes have been sat upon
more than once, but otherwise all is well.
Seriously regarded—the only thing I hate in a
“ Gaiety Girl ” is the persistent way they peg at
the fact that “society” shudders at the mere
mention of a burlesque actress as a friend for its
daughter—culminating in Decima Moore recon-
ciling, on the kiss-and-be-friends principle, Gaiety
gal with society belle and vice-versa in a ridicu-
lous denouement. Florence Lloyd hurls rage and
defiance at society with enough realistic bitter-
ness to make the dress-circle bristle with annoy-
ance, or else smile complacently to think that the
burlesque actress feels so very strongly about it
all. I really fail to see why the vexed ques-
tion re the social status of the lady who wears
tights should be so suddenly and desperately dealt
with. The sparkling “Gaiety Girl” plays with no
mission to reconcile the stage and society, I hope.
If she does, it’s foredoomed to failure, for the mis.
sionesses are far too pretty to make any femal e
converts. A knock-kneed, hollow-eyed band, with
no powder on their noses and flat cheeks, are tha
only beings who might effect something.
But it takes more than one pretty girl fiercely
declaiming in an abbreviated bathing-suit to
undermine the inherent snobbishness of human
nature. Re Gov. Hampden’s appointment, a
Sydney evening newspaper remarked that, “in
pedigree, as is befitting for the Governor of the
senior province, Lord Hampden can give Lord
Brassey points.” Perhaps so—but in point of
celebrity and known ability, Victoria’s Gov. is a
very large stack of potatoes compared with the
incoming N.S.W. Gov., who is by no means rich,
either. Gov. Brassey, even if he did hot possess
a fortune of several solid millions, would probably
have made a name or a noise In the world, being
a man with some brains and solid Hing'ish
character. N.S.W.’s brand-new Brahmin is
descended roundaboutly from John Hampden,
who was the friend of Parkes 200 or 300 years
ago; but he isn’t in any way a distinguished man.
As someone remarked, “ So far as we know he is
only ‘a village Hampden.’” However, all that
doesn’t matter a row of pins—the question is:
“Will he give nice tea-parties, and plenty of
them ?”
As feminine garb becomes more mannish, mas-
culine raiment, it would seem, grows “prettier,”
more elaborate, and more milliner-made. Witness
the rustling silk-lined coats, the evidently tongs-
curled hair, and the elaborate cravats of to-day.
A further step in the direction of girlish triviality
in male attire has been made by Aubrey Beards-
ley who, in his newest photographs, wears a Soft,
full shirt-front of fine lawn, and, in place of the
stiff collar of commerce, a point lace This
style, it is said, is catching on in “artistic circles,”
and it is only a question of time till it reaches
Johnnie, Chappie & Co.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The news of the death of Mr. J. A. Scarr, the
well-known A.J.O. handioapoer, though not unexpected,
has been received with much regret by his numerous
friends in England, writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent. Mr. Scarr was first prompted to take up the
subject of handicapping by his association with the late
Mr. W. M'Evilly, who was N.S.W. Parliamentary
Librarian, and raced, as some people still remember,
under the name of “ Mr. O’Malley." It is curious to re-
member what N.S.W. Government officials could do in
those days. M'Evilly was allowed to keep his race-horses
at the Parliament stables, and they were attended to by
members of the staff of attendants, of whom the well-
known Riley was his principal henchman. Every day one
could find either in poor Soarr’s, or J. J. Calvert’s, room,
a gathering of Government officials who talked racing by
the hour. It was then that Scarr began to practise the
art (for art it is) which he afterwards so much adorned. In
those days hon. members liked to have a flutter themselves,
and none of M'Evilly’shorses ever went to the post without
carrying heaps of their money, for they knew he would
give them a good run. How things are altered 1 What
chance would the present librarian have of being able to
train a Yattendon at, practically, the public expense?
But there were no Cricks in those days, and, if
there had been, means would have been found to keep
them quiet. But, all the same, one cannot but wonder
how the little thing was allowed to go on. It must be re-
membered, though, that Mr. Lackey (as he then was) was
an influential member of the Assembly, and combined
parliamentary duties with handicapping, whilst Speaker
Arnold was a thorough “ sport.”

will doubtless soon put the pen through The Harvester’s
name in Melb. Cup. In the Admiral (9st.), however, may

second in Sydney Cup with 9st. 91b. must not be forgot-
ten. In the last Doncaster Handicap The Admiral showed
that he had not lost his speed.

well treated.

PROSPECTUS
OF THE
Manly Co-Operative Steam Ferry
Company, Limited.
Registered under the Companies Act, whereby the
liability of a shareholder is limited to the amount
of his shares.
Capital (authorised), £20,000, in 20,000
shares of £1 each.
Capital (paid-up), £1665, on 1666 shares.
Directors:
JOHN A. PATTERSON, Esq., Chairman.
S. C. SADLER, Esq.
JOSEPH GERMAN, Esq.
W. SMART, Esq.
G. L. RICHARDSON, Esq.
Bankers:
THE LONDON BANK OF AUSTRALIA,
Limited.
Solicitors:
Messrs, LAURENCE and M‘LACHLAN,
Mutual Life Buildings, Sydney.
Registered Office:
No. 5 JETTY, CIRCULAR QUAY.
Secretary:
W. G. GERMAN.
The Manly Co-operative Steam Ferry Company
having been established 18 months, and proved to
be a paying concern, now invites applications for i
15,000 contributing shares, in order to procure |
steamers thoroughly suitable for the Manly ferry
traffic—superior to anything yet in the port, with
increased speed and accommodation—so as to cope
with the rapidly developing business of the com-
pany. . I
The Directors are convinced that, by placing
superior steamers upon the line, even greater sue- |
cess will be achieved, whilst a large proportion of
the money now being expended for charter of i
vessels will be saved; this, alone, providing a
handsome return upon the capital invested.
Arrangements have been concluded by which
the Minister for Lands has consented to enlarge
the present wharf accommodation at Manly, thus
providing suitable wharfage for this company.
The assets of the company, consisting of cash
and properties, on the 30th April, amounted to —
after writing off £683 11s. sd. for preliminary
formation expenses and depreciation of properties,
and allowing for outstanding liabilities—£2727
10s. Bd., the paid-up capital being £1665 only ; a
dividend has been declared at the rate of 10 per
cent, per annum, and a bonus of £5O to the em-
ployees, while £929 ss. Bd. has been carried for-
ward to the credit of profit and loss new account,
which new shareholders will reap the advantage
of.
The Co-operative Company has the active sup-
port and sympathy of a very large majority of the
Manly residents, many of whom are shareholders,
and who are increasing their holding, whilst
numerous applications are being received from
other residents. At least 90 per cent, of the cargo
is secured by the company.
Designs of steamers by the well-known naval
architect, Mr. Walter Reeks, are in the hands of
the Directors, who will, immediately sufficient
shares have been applied for to warrant them
doing so, proceed with the building of vessels,
which will be of the most improved type, fitted
with the electric-light throughout, and all the
latest improvements that science can suggest, com-
bining speed, comfort, and safety, and capable of
running the trip in 25 minutes.
Shares are offered on the following terms : Is.
per share on application, Is. per share on allot-
ment, the balance by calls not exceeding Is. per
share at intervals of not less than one month.
Applications for 2500 shares are now in hand,
although the Directors have not pressed forward
the issue, pending the Minister’s decision re the
wharf. This having been favorably declared, early
applications for shares is necessary.
Sydney, 6th June, 1895.

Apropos , the frenzied opposition of a certain racing-man
to the “tote” is said to be due to a big bookie having a
■ay in his stable. “ Every man jumps the way his bread
is buttered.”
• * * •
“Lord Brassey is doubtful of doing much with
his weight over the Australian country, but he will not
object to have a space specially arranged with gates, and
the high rail-fence of the colony lowered so that sport
may be engaged in more on English lines.” How very,
very considerate 1
* • • •
There were two football matches on Sydney
Cricket Ground last Saturday. The Randwick-Wallaroo
match was in every way a scientific and entertaining
shindy. There was only one temporary corpse, who was
soon resurrected. The Randwicks, who are the heaviest
team this year, came on the field with a record of 57
points scored against, all previous adversaries’ nothing.
So that when Wallaroo, by better forward combination,
got a try to Randwiok’s 0 in the first half, people began
to be wise after the event. But in the second half the
premier team bore down its opponents and scored nine
points to their 0. Randwick thus won by nine points to
three.
* * • ♦
In his three last matches Deceit, of Geelong,
has notched 15 goals.
* • • *
The Essendon footballers now see that nothing
fails like failure. With their decline their best men
are leaving. “ Bob ” Byers has gone to join Thurgood at
Freemantle ; Chadwick is also going West. Johnstone
and O’Loughlin are seceding, and, lastly, Thurgood has
definitely declined to come back.
• • • •
The theory on which men are handicapped by
N.S.W. Cash Cyclists’ League is all flapdoodle. Says the
League: “Fifteen yards start is no start, because the
man in front merely acts as a pace-maker for the men
behind, and, therefore, assists the back men to beat him ;
but give a man 25 yards start, and then, then the back
man has to catch him.” And this is acted on as a big
principle, in face of the fact that the most desperate races
are ridden over and over again where one man keeps but
a yard advantage over the other. Fifteen yards is a
huge handicap ; give Lewis 15yds. start of Megson in a
mile, : and see if Megson will consider Lewis a pace-maker,
A simple experiment like that can always disprove a
stupid theory.
r * ♦ ♦ •
One fundamental objection to cash cycling is
that the majority of riders will naturally ride “ stiff" in
order to secure so big a; handicap in some future event as
to make victory and cash (not honor) certain. How many
of the present flight of cash cyclists are working this
dodge? How few are not! Mammon pollutes even men
with minds; and cyclists and other athletes are usually
physical successes with small minds or no minds at all.
The incessant idea of cash, and cash only, demoralises
them at hurricane speed.
* • * *
Thejbieycle thief in Australian cities is giving
way to the bicycle “finder.” A cyclistleavinghismachine
in the street for a few moments comes back to find it
g me. ;Next morning his machine is advertised : “Pound,
a bicycle ; finder expects reward.” On enquiry it turns
out(«o the finder says) that the said “finder” “found”
the cycle some 20 miles away, and spent all the night and
early morning in bringing it home; being too poor, &o.
The cash cyclist generally gives him a shilling or so.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Huret, the French cyclist, has ridden the won-
derfutdistance of 515 miles in 24 hours ; an average of 21$
miles per hour. The previous best was the 462-mile per-
form nice of the Englishman, Shorland. It must be borne
in mind that Shoriand’s ride was done on an uncovered
track, where the wind and night dew affected him. Huret
rode under perfect conditions—on a covered-in track.
Cycling is seriously competing with horse-racing in
France ; as many as 800 entries have been received for a
single road-race there ; and nearly every daily paper in
Paris has its cycling column.
• * * *
Huret’a 24 hours’ ride is not to be compared
from the point of sleepless cycling with G. P. Mills’ ride
from Land's End to John o’ Groats. He rode for three
days, 14 hours, and 15 min., without one moment of sleep,
without once reclining to rest. He lowered the previous
record by 9 hours 40 min., and he rode the last part of his
trip at the rate of 16 miles an hour.
♦ v * * *
Given fair play, Ted Pritchard (says The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent) would most undoubtedly
have bealeniigm Smith in their recent Holhorn battle,
but, as usual, Smith’s adherents consisted of the very
cream of ruffianism, who did their worst, and succeeded.
This is the third fight of Smith’s in which the blackguard
element has prevailed. Pritchard, it is true, is but the
shadow of the smart boxer who made himself so popular a
few years ago, but he was able for all that to give Smith
more than he got. It was a case of mere rough-and-
tumble, and when Smith happened to knock Pritchard on
to the ropes, the adherents of the quondam champion got
hold of him and held him down so that he could not get
up within the prescribed time. This should quite finish
English glove-fighting, and really one cannot wonder that
Corbett has some doubts about getting fair play in
London. Jem Smith, say what they will, is a game bit of
stuff, for he took a lot of punishment from Pritchard
without flinching. Jake Kilrain gave him enough to
knock the heart out of a lion, yet he came to the scratch
as if nothing had happened.
* • * •
Sid. Gregory, the N;S.W. cricketer, having
compiled many runs in great matches, and having earned
a goodly sum thereby, has invested his savings and his
future prospects in a tobacco, cricketing-goods, and
football business in Sydney.
* * * *
A Victorian testimonial to Harry Boyle was
only started five days before the veteran’s departure for
Bananaland, and, although he was to receive the purse of
sovs. on Saturday last, the door of the subscription-room
will, presumably, be left ajar a while longer. Boyle was
the old, reliable'bowler, the Alfred Shaw of many an Aus-
tralian and Vic. eleven. Also, he stuck to his club, the
East Melbourne, for 22 years, and behaved like a white
man all the time.

“ Orom-a-boo,” the irresistibly funny and
rollicking Irish song of the Hoben Brothers, which
Sappho Smith opined would prove “a terrible
■success,’’is already proving. Lonnen has taken
it up in London, and Lauri is to sing it in
Australia; it is to be well introduced in America,
and in Maoriland. where that clever comedian,
Percy, of the Pollard Lilliputians, is making a
feature of it- It is also a universal success and
heard every where. Paling’s have already cleared
the first big edition, and have got out two others
in E flat and F. It is certainly the funniest Irish
football-song ever written, and the press every-
where has risen to describe it as the best cojmc
song of the day, and funnier than Killaloe or
“ Ballyhooley.’’ It is just tlm thing for an aspir-
ing comique, pro. or amateur. * „

rules the world,” says Bismarck. And therefore,

Dear Bulletin, —A nut for the sex-
equality people ! The occasional alleged male who
has a weakness for paint and powder, pads and
skirts, is a sub-feminine anomaly—recognised as a
modified instance of the disease of “sex-perver-
sion.” Normal man never copies woman, whereas
normal woman—or a good many of her—imitates
man for all she’s worth. She puts three inches of
his stature under her heels, and six more on top
of her head. She lias taken his hat and his coat,
his shirt and his cigarette ; she’s reaching out for
his pants, and, if her little stomach would stand
it, she’d collar his pipe. It’s only a poor imita-
tion, after all, but it’s pretty well meant —for an
“equal 1 Ueketic.

At Castlemaine (Vic.) the other night)
three new-made knights breathed their sentiment B
over a banquet to the local bowling-club president-
J. B. Patterson said there was “nothing to keep
this fine country depressed but crass ignorance ’’ —
Protection being tlie crass ignorance he had in
view. Zeal, the figurehead of the Leg. Council,
observed that “he did not desire to have such
frivolous questions as women’s suffrage interfer-
ing with important measures;” and old Jock
MTntyre, with true statesmanlike feeling, in-
formed the banquet that “if the ladies got into the
House, lie would pair with some of them and go for
refreshments.” Three belted knights don’t often
descend upon a one-horse town at one fell swoop,
and the remarks they fire off upon such an ex-
ceptional occasion deserve careful record.
At the recent Dublin Agricultural Show—
the most important event of its kind in Ireland—
the gold medal offered for the best cream-separator
was awarded to the Alpha “ B.” machine, which
separated 66 gallons of cream in an hour. The
“ Humming Bird ” separator, another machine of
the Alpha type and by the same makers, Was also
specially commended as the best for small dairies.
The Alpha separators have secured a long series
of prizes at many different shows, the Dublin
award being the latest. Messrs. Waugh and
Josephson, of 342 Sussex-street, are the Sydney
agents for these valuable appliances.
In a chemical factory in Alsace, lately—
according to a ghoulsoine London paper—a work-
man fell into a vat of sulphuric acid, and was
entirely dissolved along with his clothes. At
least, he went a-mis>ing, and the above result was
afterwards deduced from some buttons found in
the vat. This event seems to open out a lot of
new possibilities for the novelist, whose villain is
always greatly embarrassed with “ the body.”
Even though he throws it overboard in mid-ocean,
or buries it in a cave and weighs it down with
stones, it insists on turning up again in the third
volume. What is wanted is something that will
utterly obliterate and blot out “ the body,” and
then the way of the villain will be easy, and the
hero won’t always make us feel mad by recovering
the property in the last chapter.
REMORSE.
To know that I have sinned ; to feel all day,
r 1 And every day, the scourger’s lash that cuts
The deeper that its marks are hid away
From human sight. To know that one soul shuts
Henceforth the door of joy and peace and love
Upon itself through me and me alone;
And hopes for nothing more below, above,
r Except to quickly die—making no moan
To call for the pity for a broken life—
Giving me no reproach for that base part
That I did play to win—unequal strife —
A devil’s science ’gainst an angel’s heart.
To know all this, and yet live on—ah, well!
No need to wait for death to taste of Hell!
L. E. L.

Admiral Hornby, who died recently at the
age of 70, was commonly described as “ the
greatest sailor of our time.” Yet be was only
twice in action in his life. In his boyhood he was
present at the bombardment of the crumbling
town of Acre. And in his old age, when he com-
manded the British fleet in the Sea of Marmora,
he sent a boat’s crew ashore to burn and demolish
the illicit grog-shop of a certain Greek who sold
bad liquor to Ins sailors. Hornby didn’t actually
take a hand in this exploit, but as the burnt-out
Greek stood on the shore and shook a stick at him
it may be charitably set down as the second time
he saw active service.
There’s nothing makes a chap so satisfied
with himself as meeting in after-years the woman
who refused to marry him when she had the
chance.— Fkank#obt Moobe.

In Oldham (England) and its immediate
neighborhood there are 93 cotton-spinning com-
panies, In 1890, eight of these worked at a loss ;
in ’9l, 49 of them ; in ’92, 57; in ’93, 63; and in
’94, no less than 67, so that only 26 are paying
their way. Alleged cause, the competition of the
cheap Bombay nigger and the Jap. (for the trade
in their own countries).
“Prahran, the second city of Victoria,’
is senator Gray’s modest way of describing the
place which returned him to Parliament at last
general election. Melbourne, including Prahran,
is the first city of Victoria, and Prahran, exclud-
ing all other component cities of the first city, is
the second city. What paltry nonsense is this
provincial craze for calling every suburb a city !
One metropolis—one city.

Mr. Nathan Herman, of the Misfit Par-
lors, No. 9 Wynyard-street, Sydney—the place
where the big banner is, and where the immense
“9” looks like a frozen boa-constrictor over the
door, and the huge lamp serves as a beacon to
guide the wandering footsteps of the pilgrim from
the bush —has vastly increased his business within
the last few weeks. A mammoth new depart-
ment has been added for the sale of furniture,
crockery, pianos, bedding, ironmongery, &c., and
the young man who is going to get married, and
be afterwards run-in on his mother-in-law’s evi-
dence, can here find absolutely everything that
he wants. A new feature of the jewellery depart-
ment is the reliable watch, with gilt chain which
looks like gold—Ml for 6s. fid. The misfit tobacco
and cigar department has been greatly enlarged,
and all kinds of plugs are now supplied. Herman’s
tea is a wonderful speciality. The dress and
mantle department has also greatly expanded, and
a large staff of ladies—most of them young and
beautiful—are kept on hand to make all necessary
alterations while you wait. Sundry large ship-
ments of Brussels carpets have arrived, and are
being sold at 2s. a yard, with no charge whatever
for making and laying; and various consignments
of rugs, blankets, boots, shoes, overcoats, water-
proofs, &0., &c., are being sold at the same ruinous
rates. Also, the four winds which blow in the
halls of heaven continue to bring in orders from
everywhere for the famous £2 parcel, which in-
cludes a suit of clothes, two boiled shirts, two
under-shirts, six pairs of socks, two ties, two
collars, six handkerchiefs —no person with the
kind of cold which prevails just now can get
through with less than six—set of studs, hat, pair
braces and a pair of boots. The said orders, each
with remittance attached, now average 100 daily,
and many letters expressing the satisfaction of
the purchasers can be seen at the office. A large
consignment of misfits in every branch of clothing,
and bought at merely nominal prices, has just
been received per Orotava, and Mr. Herman is
shortly starting for Europe to make contracts for
the purchase of all misfits from the leading tailors
of London, Glasgow, Liverpool, Paris, Vienna,
and other cities. When this arrangement is com-
pleted, he calculates that the business will be the
most extensive of its kind south of the line.
Meanwhile, an additional elevator is being put in;
and the trade is already slopping over far beyond
the limits of the original premises, and Mr. Her-
man is concocting schemes for absorbing the whole
block from George-st. right down to York-st.

Baron Brain well, one of the ablest Judges
that ever adorned the English bench, a few years
ago, at the celebration of his 50th legal anniver-
sary, said that of accusations by women against
men which had come under his notice during his
long career, fully 70 per cent, were false, and of
the remainder many were not proven. “ Many
legislators, in amiable imbecility, took the part of
women against men ; but it was really the men
who needed-protection.”
Min Lee and Ming Sam were neighbours
near Bendigo, Vic. Min was married, Ming was
not ; and one day, when Min’s attention was
diverted, Ming carried off Mrs. Lee and estab-
lished her in his own home. Min subsequently
discovered the fraud, but offered no complaint.
A couple of weeks later Ming Sam returned the
lady, whereupon Min Lee went forth and clubbed
him nearly to death with a rake-handle. Six
months. And this is a free country !

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The other day a Queensland Minister of
the Crown had just taken his seat in a reserved
carriage when two ladies walked in. They had
golden hair, large white feathers in their hats,
and soft, lustrous eyes that “flashed on you a
volley of rays that said a thousand things at once.”
They smiled on the Minister with a brace of
smiles sweet as the honey of Mount Hybla, and
“hoped they were not intruding.” Just then a
cold-blooded guard, with grey eyes, a sharp nose,
and thin lips, rushed up and called the two ladies,
somewhat imperatively, to “come into another
carriage !”
• • *
In 1870, when W. H. Walsh was Minister
for Railways, he ordered that no women of im-
moral character were to be allowed on the rail-
ways, and stationmasters were actually instructed
not to grant tickets to any member of Phryne’a
giddy brigade. The refusal of a ticket to an ex-
governess of clean reputation promised to create
very unpleasant complications, and the insane
orders were promptly cancelled. An account of
the actual origin of the anti-Lais regulations
would be too sweet for anything.
• • •
No prouder man than the late Hon. Louis
Hope ever stood on Australian soil. His family
pride was really a monomania of a kind common
to the “old nobility” of both civilised and
savage races. It was similar to that of the Khan
of Tartary who, after his dinner of mare’s milk
and brown bread, ordered a skin-clad ruffian to
proclaim that all the princes and kings of the
earth had now his gracious permission to go and
dine. Hope started to form the first Q. sugar-
plantation in ’63, at Cleveland, 25 miles from
Brisbane. One day, when engaged in hoeing cane,
he was visited by Gov. Bowen, Lord Onslow and
Premier Herbert. Herbert walked up to Hope
and told him of the two distinguished visitors
who had come to look over the estate. “Very
well, show them round !” said Hope, not even
looking up or stopping his hoe. He once sum-
moned the late Murray Prior for taking a pine-
apple, but the case was “settled out of court.”
At first he wanted to make it a charge of larceny,
but the idea of Murray Prior stealing a pine-apple
evoked giggles from even the police-magistrate’s
dog.
* # #
One of the early Queensland Governors
was suspected of somnambulism whenever the
handsome wife of a well-known official of that
date was a guest at Government House. In order
to dispel or confirm the unworthy suspicion, one
of the domestics was bribed to sprinkle a layer of
dry flour in front of the Governor’s bedroom door

The recent resignation of the commander-
in-ohiefship of the “Army of Queensland,” by
Lieut.-001. E. R. Drury, C.M.G.—haw—recalls
one or two reminiscences of that famous eye-
glassed warrior whose financial genius the
Q.N.B. rewards with a salary of £3500. Drury
was a smart and plucky athlete in his young years,
and scored three points out of nine in a bayonet
v • sword combat with the _ famous Professor
Parker, at Brisbane, in the sixties. (Parker, by the
way, was the original of Dickens’Sergeant George
’ n 14 Bleak House ”). Drury was also an unpleasant
person with his bare hands. He was accustomed
to lunch at a hotel that stood on the site of the
Courier office. One day, while walking round
the table, before the bell rang, surveying the
dishes through his eye-glass, a Darling Downs
squatter came in and followed him round, looking
through a stirrup-iron, and finally gazed super-
ciliously in Drury’s face and remarked—“ Haw !”
Drury quietly said, “ I see you want to insult me?”
U e squatter said it might be taken in that
light if Drury so desired. “Ah, then we will go
outside,” said Drury, and they adjourned to the
backyard, where the stirrup-iron man was badly
knocked-out in the first round with a black eye
an£ f a damaged nose, and feelings wounded to an
extent beyond compensation. But many years of
turkey and cabs have changed Drury, and one
flush hit on the binjie would create a vacancy for
a bank-manager at <£2ooo per annum—because
nobody but Drury would ever receive £3500 in
tbese days-

A few miles from Brisbane there is a
hotel-keeper who has loaded himself to the muzzle
with dictionaries, and has a cheerful habit of
going off unexpectedly and scattering weird ortho-
graphical missiles in the wrong directions. Having
a bad cold recently, he told a visitor that “ My
barronical tubes are digested ! ” “ What portion
of your respiratory anatomy are those in?” said
the visitor. "None of your sarsikasms ! ”
grunted mine host; “ do you think I know all the
farmerscopia! ”
• » *
One of the members of the present Q. Par-
liament kept a small tobacconist’s shop in Mary-
borough back in the sixties. One day a black gin
walked in and wanted some cheap tobacco. The
future M.L.A. ordered her out and presented a
revolver to emphasize his request. The revolver
went off, the ball passed through the gin and she
staggered out and died on the footpath. The jury
returned a verdict of nob guilty, on the ground
that the affair was an accident—which it doubtless
was. But it is, or ought to be, a crime to point
firearms, loaded or unloaded, at your fellow-
citizen.

In the history of Queensland scientific dis-
covery there is one incident enveloped in a thick
fog. When Carl Theodore Staiger, now dead
some years, was Curator of the Museum he visited
Gayndah, on the Burnett River. At breakfast he
remarked'that “dis vas a very sthrange feesh on
mein plate !” There is a dark suspicion that this
piscatorial curiosity was skilfully fabricated by one
or two local conspirator jokists out of parts of a
eeratodus and two more common fishes, m order to
impose on the Teutonic scientist. Certain enough
is the fact that Staiger sent a fragment to Count
Castelnau, who actually named the supposed
original fish as “ Ompax spatuloides,” which
name is duly recorded in the list of Q’sland
fishes. Equally certain is the fact that no speci-
men of the ompax was ever seen before, and no-
body has ever seen one since. Perhaps Mr.
Illidge, the Gayndah postmaster and local ichthyo-
logist, can lighten the dark places of this Staigerian
mystery ?
MAROOGALINE.
Melbournian : ’ “Robertson and Moffat
always show beautiful autotypes, and to-day
Dagnan-Bouveret’s Madonna entrances every be-
holder. How superior to the maudlin Italian impos-
sibilities ! The child, swaddled, hides his face,
snuggling to the cheek of the divinely beautiful
mother, clad in coarse, white samite, as she walks
under a gloriously sunlit vinery.”
The (English) Royal Niger Company has
grabbed in Africa a territory of about 500 000
square miles, containing a population of about 20
millions. Rhodes’ British South Africa Go. has
direct control over 125,000 square miles, and lays
claim to four times that area ; while the Imperial
British East Africa Co. claims sovereign rights
over no less than 750,000 square miles. Here,
then, are three chartered companies which have
extended British dominion over areas six times as
large as N.S. Wales!
The overbuilt condition of Melb. outlying
suburbs is partly evinced by the number of private
wooden buildings carted to and re-erected in other
districts during the last year. The Vie, Govern-
ment has adopted the idea of shifting old
buildings, and having found it much cheaper—in
one case—than building a new school, has just
accepted tenders for removing a ten-roomed school
building from dunes, and re-erecting it at Pern
Tree Gully, 200 miles away. Boom buildings are
now so cheap that it costs 50 per cent, less to carry
one 200 miles and put it up again, than it would
to erect the same building in the usual way.
Broken Hill has at present perhaps the
cheapest meat-supply in Australia. Mutton car-
cases are regularly auctioned singly, bringing
from Is. 9d. to 4s. 6d. each, families generally
combining to purchase. A Poolamacca pastoralisb
who originated the idea sends a truck-load of car-
cases daily by Tarrawingee tram (38 miles.) The
local butchers, who previously retailed mutton at
4d. per lb. and beef up to 9d., have brought prices
down to a nominal figure in consequence, bub
threaten to make up during the hob months when
the meat will not travel.
Bards of the Baekblocks.
THE BELLBIRD RUNG HER HOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah! ’twas God-time in September, in that per
fumed hazel belt,
Where the musk-leaves, thick and waxen, from
their two sides throw the scent,
And the supplejack’s star blossoms in the endless
dewings melt—
When a bird said “ Love ” so often that a child
knew what he meant ;
When cream-cheeked Polly Ryan made her angel
eyes terrene
As I told her of the load of everlasting love I
bore,
And her glance made Heaven seem open, and the
roadside’s dewy sheen
Was to me as pearls which never grew, but ar e
for evermore.
And she said, “ Your kiss is folly !”
Bub she meant it not, rny Polly,
And we kissed adown that roadside, past the
grass edge and its loam,
And, deep from glen of fern,
As we loitered at the turn,
The bellbird rung us from the kiss—the bellbird
rung us home!
Oh ! ’bis death-time in this March time, but the
bellbird rings his bell—
In glad deceit from mossy floors the silver note
he sends,
And the sky is hodden grey and my heart it is i»
hell!
And bub another bellbird to the past a memory
lends;
And death-cheeked Polly Ryan no more hath
eyes terrene.
For her eyes are stars in Heaven, and their
glance the night winds bear !
And to me no heaven is open ! On her grave the
dewy sheen
Is as pearls that faintly glisten in the dusk of
my despair!
And no more my kiss is “ folly,”
For Death’s kiss hath dumbed sweet Polly,
And the bellbird that hath rung her to the grave,
edge, and its loam
Calls no more from glen of fern—
For she left me at the turn ;
And the bellbird rung us from the kiss—the bell,
bird rung her home !
Sydney.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Emily Faithfull, lately deceased, was the
English “New Woman” of the early sixties, not
an advocate of breeches and bikes for the sex, but
a preacher of woman’s right to compete with man
in certain remunerative fields of industry. In her
young days Emily Faithfull was popularly re-
garded as a “crank.” She lived to be a com-
parative Tory among the advanced sisterhood, and
Gladstone pub her on the civil-pension list for ser-
vices rendered to the State. Her new ideas on
the relations between the sexes didn’t extend out-
side the labor question.

on
In the Ward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It wanted a quarter of an hour to midnight. The
stillness of the casualty-ward was only broken
now and then by a groan—the miserable language
of pain. The night-nurse had just come on duty.
She sat at a small table at the end of the, long
room, with an open book before her in which the
names and particulars of the cases received during
the day were carefully noted for her guidance. A
shaded lamp at her elbow threw a subdued light
upon the page ; and, as she turned her head to
listen to the heavy breathing of a patient, her pale
face, looking out from the circle of the rays, took
the likeness of a ghostly mask suspended in mid-
air.
A tall, dark-haired woman, with deep eyes that
shone, black-rimmed, amid the pallor of her face.
She wore a wedding-ring, the relic of a husband
she had deserted, of a child she had abandoned.
In one type of woman repentance finds expression
in some form of self-flagellation. Necessity makes
the wife, monotony discovers the woman, and
remorse produces the hospital-nurse.
Midnight striking, she moved up the long alley
■between the bed-rows ; here administering a dose
of medicine, there cooling a parched throat,
smoothing a pillow, and adjusting a bandage;
and so down the dim-lit ward to the table again.
There was one patient yet to be visited. A bed
isolated from the rest contained the latest case,
brought in that night. Its serious nature was
shown by the screen drawn around - often a sign
that the bed will be vacant by morning.
The nurse stepped to the table and made some
marginal notes in the book, then referred to the
last entry : “ Man run over—compound fracture
of the right thigh-bone—chest crushed in—exces-
sive loss of blood —vitality alcoholically impaired.”
She read the minute directions as to treatment at
the foot of the entry, and turned to pass round
the screen.
Suddenly a man entered noiselessly by the little
door communicating with the receiving-room, and
laid a hand on her arm. The nurse recognised
the house-surgeon. They stood together in the
shadow. On the edge of the table and on the floor
at their feet the lamp made a white splash of light;
beyond was the semi-obscurity of the ward, with
its vista of beds and the dim outlines of the
figures upon them. Through the silence the
labored breathing of the man behind the screen
came heavily in hoarse, convulsive spasms.
The doctor said something in an undertone.
He was a short, thick-set man, with a full under-
lip. The nurse shook her head slightly, and made
an irresolute motion in the direction of the screen.
The man caught her round the waist, and whis-
pered eagerly in her ear. The nurse’s deep eyes
sparkled, and a flush crept into her pale face. The
stiff folds of the white apron she wore rustled
gently as they rose and fell upon her breast. The
close atmosphere of the ward was charged with
human electricity. Through the half-open door
of the receiving-room filtered an inviting breath
of cool air, lightly stirring a stray hair upon the
nurse’s neck. With a movement gentle but
irresistible the man drew her to him.
At that moment, in the very instant that her
will yielded to his, the heavy breathing behind
the screen ceased suddenly. The silence roused
her as a sleeper rouses at the stopping of a clock.
The instinct of the nurse overcame that of the
woman. Picking up the lamp, she hurried across
the floor and turned back the screen. The man
lay huddled and still, with his knees drawn up and
one hand clenched on the coverlet. His eyes were
wide open, and she saw at a glance that he was
dead. She bent over the bed so that the light fell
upon his face.
It was her husband.
With a strange little shuddering gasp she passed
into the receiving-room with the doctor.
Melbourne.
BERBA ESPINASSE.
On May 26 telegrams were sent for experi-
mental purposes from Derby, W.A., to Cape
York, Q., (7341 miles, the world’s record.) The
messages were distinct and altogether successful.
A fossilised Chinaman has been unearthed
near Wyndham, Westralia. The Argus corres-
pondent remarks cheerfully that “ the skin was
strong enough to allow of boat-hooks being thrust
through to lift the body, and attempts to cut it
with a spade were futile.” How nice !
An unbeliever arose at the first of Coun-
tess Wachbcoeister’s Melb. spook-lectures and
blandly inquired if she had experienced any of the
strange Mahatmio manifestations she was so
glib in connecting with H. P. Blavatsky. If so,
would she relate them. Replied the wily Coun-
tess : “ I make it a rule never to relate my per-
sonal experiences. I don’t want to be persecuted.”
Whereat the audience distinctly winked.
Look out for a revival of novels in which
the secret poisoner uses arsenic. The vogue for
the last few years has been to make aconite the
toxic agent. But, there are known to the practical
chemist since alkaloids in conjunction with basic
salts (for readiness in dissolving), have come into
general use, one or two absolutely colorless solu-
tions, a few or many drops of which would mean
a lingering death or an apparent stroke by light-
ning.
Seymour (Vic.) was lately troubled with a
violent lunatic who had a “mission.” His strong
point consisted in seizing small children on their
way to school and baptising them under a tap.
When he had baptised a terrified small boy he
would recite sundry passages of Scripture to his
drenched victim, and then turn him loose to take
hia moist and squashy way to his destination.
The lunatic was captured the other day while
holding forth to a small, soared inhabitant and
bobbing him up and down in a horse-trough for
the good of his soul, and peace is restored once
more.
A Bulletin man sate in a Melb. bar one
recent evening waiting to have a prescription
made up, when a nigger entered. The colored
man was barefooted, wore an old billycock, and a
blue jumper; he carried an absurd boomerang
under his arm, and, aS he entered, drew into view
from his breast a brass crescent inscribed “Jaoky,”
The bar was almost full of young fellows, and to
these the nig. pitched a woeful tale. He wanted
to sell the boomerang, also two alleged spear-
heads, and two bone tie-guards said to have been
carved out of a human fragment. The crowd was
touched, and God’s image in ebony realised 12/6
for his stock, and an enthusiastic young Austra-
lian provided him with half a tumbler of rum.
After he had gone, the conversation turned upon
the poor Australian aboriginals and their rapid
extinction, but the Bulletin man abstained from
dropping a sympathetic tear, frr the simple reason
that the “ native ” who had just left was a woolly-
headed, thick-necked, thick-lipped African black.
Amongst the important news of the day
in Wimmera Star (Vic.) the following occupies a
prominent place : “ We have been informed that,
owing to extremely urgent pressure having been
brought to bear on the popular landlords of the
White Hart and Royal Hotels, and owing to cir-
cumstances over which they had no control, they
have decided to reduce the price of beer to 3d.”
“We have been informed !” Superb diplomatist!
Certain Yic. sea-shore municipalities are
at present engaged in a fierce struggle for the
“honor” of receiving Gov. Brassey. For ven-
turing to suggest that Brassey should put in at
Brighton, and that the Mayor should wade into
the water and carry him ashore, a democratic
councillor was severely reprimanded last week.
Notwithstanding, a Port Melbourne councillor
next night expressed the popular sentiment on the
subject by saying that “the people didn’t care a
hang if the Governor were landed at Timbuctoo,
and brought on to Melbourne by flying-machine.”
Horrible !
The Duke of Hamilton waa only in hia
jubilee year when he died, t’other day, yet he
dated back, as a turf-plunger, to the Marquis of
Hastings era. He started to ruin himself in his
teens, but had the good luck to become so deeply
indebted to Pad wick, the money-lender, that he
couldn’t get rid of his property elsewhere. It
suited Padwick to assume a paternal control of
the young duke and his belongings. Hamilton
went abroad for a time, and lived at comparatively
mild expense, whilst Padwick pulled things round
for his obedient pupil and guarded him from rob-
bery by anyone save “dear old Paddy.” The
brainy usurer was, under the circumstances, the
best possible steward for the family estates.
At 30 years of age the Duke of Hamilton
found himself able to “go it ” as of yore, and
he has been going it on fairly rational lines
ever since. He wasn’t born with much brains —
the son of a father who broke his neck by falling
down a flight of steps when drunk, and a mother
who sought consolation in amours with footmen
during her husband’s lifetime—but fat Hamilton
had brains enough co know that Padwick was his
preserver, and act accordingly. A good many
yarns concerning this double-barrelled peer and
hereditary potentate have appeared in The Bul-
letin during the past 10 years. Any one of them
will serve as decent argument for the abolition of
the House of Lords. In his later bachelor days—
i.e., after the property had been “nursed,” but
before he got married—the Duke of Hamilton
would stay for weeks together at Newmarket, a
gloomy spot in the winter, and haunt the house of
his then trainer, Tom Brown. Mrs. Brown found
his Grace a great trial as a guest until she fortu-
nately thought of giving him a “solitaire” board to
amuse himself with. “Solitaire” was a fashionable
game for the young in those days. You had a
board and a number of glass marbles occupying
circular holes therein, and you fooled about with
your marbles until only one remained—solitaire-
in the centre hole. Any intelligent boy could
master the art of bringing this game to a success-
ful finish, but the Duke of Hamilton pegged away
hour after hour and never learned how to leave
the last marble in the middle of the board. He
was the premier duke of Scotland, and many more
things beside, but he confessed to his trainer’s
wife at the end of 10 weeks that “ solitaire ” put
too great a strain upon his intellect. It was a
“fair licker,” said his Grace.
